“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly— Australla’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
It the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy .
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction,
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. *
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which |
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent |
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every i
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the ela’ms of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Ear North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
SPECIAL NOTICE.
Subscription (payable strictly in advance). Six
Shillings and Sixpence per Quarter, or One Pound
per Annum.
Business communications to be addressed to
William Maoleod, Managing Director, to whom
all remittances are payable. Editorial communi.
ations to be addressed to J. E. Archibald.

The Winner of the Squatters’ Cup.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Luck good ? Yes, pretty fairish now, the worst I
ever knew,
Came when I won—and lost—a race for Scott at
Wallaroo.
How’s that ? you say—well, if you like, I’ll try to
make it clear :
’Twas in the spring of Ninety, in the grand old Car-
bine’s year,
I rode Scott’s well-known Planet, and I didn’t
care a rap,
Bar Sheik, for all the entries in the Squatters’
Handicap.
(A fine, big bay like Planet was that Dick Delaney’s
Sheik,
A blooming muff had got the mount —Bill Long,
of Sandy Creek.)
’Twas over rails and water, too, the district’s
favourite race—
My word ! the Cup and Stakes were grand for
such a one-pub. place.
That year they’d capped the fences, but the
stewards most were proud
Of that deep, muddy water-jump they’d scooped
to please the crowd.
Down dropped the flag, and in the lead abreast
raced Bill and I;
Abreast we cleared the first three jumps. “Sheik!
Planet ! Sheik ! ’’ they cry.
Those mulga cappings on the “logs’’had pulled
the others up,
And one of us, the public knew, must win the
Squatters’ Cup ;
Justus I thought I’d be that one, there came a
sudden fear,
Bill’s prad was racing fresh and strong, while mine
rolled blooming queer :
And I cursed the keyless stables, way back there
in the bush.
They’d “ got at ” dear old Planet then, that blank
Delaney push.
No ! P’r’aps I’d better not explain, how with an
ugly thud,
We jostled at the water-jump, and fell in soupy
mud,
We lost our reins, and Bill got kicked, by what he
didn’t know,
(But when six pair of legs get mixed, a young ’un
gets a show).
We scrambled out two yellow jocks, each caught
a trailing rein.
We sprang on yellow horses, and we raced away
again;
“ Sheik ! Sheik ! ” they yell. Bill got the start,
they knew him by his hair
(Housed to sport a ragged “mo,” my face was
then quite bare).
My mount seemed freshened up a lot, he gained
at ev’ry stride.
And then there came another yell, “ Old Planet
wins !” they cried ;
Unheeded fell Bill’s cruel whip, I saw his prad
was done,
I passed his flanks, his girth, his head—the
Squatter’s Cup was won!
“You wretched fool,” said Scott to me (but not a
word I spoke—
I knew the boss’s larking ways—l thought I saw
his joke);
“ It’s no good my protesting, for you weighed in
both the same ”
(Just here I winked to let him know I twigged
his little game).
“You wretched fool,” said he, again, with more
dramatic force,
“You think you won ! Blank you, you did ! —on
Sheik, Delaney's horse!”
FRANK BELLMAN.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take
what are vour peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS is,

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which yim may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
tn the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not ivish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor trill carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence’’ column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 toords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
Bat we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission oj such MSS.

2S
SATURDAY, AUGUST 4, 1894.
Payment of Members.
The late New South Wales general election
was remarkable for many things, but most of
all for the multiplicity of candidates who
went to the polls. It was further remark-
able for the number of candidates who
wanted to go to the polls, but were induced,
under extreme pressure, to remain away.
Had all the aspirants tried their fortunes on
the day of election the total number of com-
petitors would not have been far short of
1000, and of this vast assemblage hardly
one remained away voluntarily ; those who
retired only did so because the central
authority of their party refused them its
support, and their chance was consequently
hopeless. Of all these seekers after legis-
lative “ honours ” it may safely be assumed
that an enormous proportion were actuated
exclusively by a desire for the salary which
goes along with the billet of M.L.A. ; their
numbers were so vastly in excess of those
who competed in the days of unpaid Legis-
latures that, even when the abolition of the
£4O deposit is duly regarded, the salary must
have been accountable for much of the differ-
ence. A considerable number also wanted the
position altogether irrespective of the salary,
but not being men of independent means
could not have held it had there been no
emoluments attached. And the balance
consisted partly of men who would have run
for Parliament had there been no payment,
but who will gladly accept the £3OO a year all
the same; and partly of men who would have
run for Parliament in any case, because
they make a dishonest income out of politics
and live on the game, and who will take
the £3OO per annum while it is going, but
would much rather it were not going, because
it brings too many competitors into the field
and thus renders their seats and their
swindles eminently precarious. There also
used to be one man—only one—who was
never suspected of dishonesty, and who
didn’t draw his salary, presumably through
conscientious motives, but as he has gone
out of New South Wales politics he need not
be included in this category.
• • •
In an Assembly thus constructed it is a
truly surprising circumstance that half,
if not more than half, the members
have announced themselves hostile to the
principle of payment of members. The
country was flooded with candidates, most
of them falling over each other in a frantic
rush to get £3OO per annum, and yet an
immense proportion of these professed to
want, above all things, the abolition of the
salary which they struggled so desperately to
secure. The community the dull, un-
thinking, unreasoning mass of humanity
which is always longing for some new
confidence-man to come along and take
it in—accepted their assurances as a solid
evidence of disinterestedness, and sent
them into Parliament accordingly, and they
gave the said assurances mainly because
they Avere a handy means of getting into
Parliament. Some 60 or 70 men now in the
Assembly arrived there—in part at least—
because they declared that they really and
truly wanted to toil at an exacting profession
for absolutely nothing ; to give up, either
wholly or in a great measure, the occupations
by which they make their living, and to get
no recompense AA T hatever ; to spend long
days and nights in weary debate ; to sit up to
the small hours of the morning keeping a
quorum for the benefit of their party, or
waiting for a division ; to snatch fitful spells
of unrefreshing slumber on the seats of the
House when they dared not go to bed lest
their votes should be Avanted at a moment’s
notice to save their bleeding country ; to be
abused by hostile newspapers and hostile
partisans ; to leave their homes, in many
instances, for six or eight months in the
year, and incur the heavy expense of living
in the metropolis—and all from unalloyed
patriotism, and with no desire for any return
whatever. To the man with no means, who
depends for his livelihood on his own exer-
tions, to be an unpaid member of Parlia-
ment —provided he doesn’t pay himself
by directly or indirectly looting the
Treasury —means a loss of at least £2OO per
annum. And Avhen 300 or 400 struggling
men will be found to voluntarily contribute,
out of their own narrow means, £2OO each
per annum to aid the Treasury when it is in
an unusually tight place, then the 300 or
400 politicians who lately professed a desire
to serve their country gratis will be genuine
—and not till then.
* * *
The reason why so many of the “ leading”
politicians have declared for a return to the
old fraudulent system is sufficiently obvious.
Until payment of members was introduced,
these individuals held office (at £l5OO a year)
turn about, and thus realised an average
salary of £750 per annum. Also, they en-
joyed, turn about, the command of vast
political patronage, which enabled them to
provide for their friends and relatives, and
to do many other things which were in-
directly worth, to an honest Minister, at
least as much as his salary, and, to a dis-
honest one, perhaps ten times as much. The
£3OO a year is very little as compared to these
privileges, and yet that honorarium has helped
into existence a third party which may, at
no distant date, snatch them all away.
Those of the rank-and-file of the old parties
who lived by straight and unadulterated
fraud, by using their Parliamentary position
to compass mysterious land-swindles, to get
roads and railways made through their
private estates, or to manipulate concessions
for syndicates and companies, who hired out
their names to shady joint-stock enterprises,
or took direct bribes for services rendered,
also lose by the present arrangement. So
do the softgoodsmen who go into Parliament
to see that no duties are levied on their
imported calico, and the brewers who go
there to vote against the beer-tax, and the
landlords who go there to vote against the
land-tax. The honorarium is very little com-
pared to the incomes which intelligent politi-
cians used to make by underhand devices ;
yet the rush of candidates which it induces
has already lost many of them their seats,
and promises to wreck most of the survivors
at a future date. Therefore, the sur-
vivors are against payment of members
almost to a man. And, as already men-
tioned, many of the new members profess
to be against it, because a dull and stupid
community was ready to take their profes-
sion as a proof of patriotism and disinter-
estedness. It was, therefore, good policy to
so profess, and they professed accordingly,
with a firm conviction that a kindly majority
would prevent the disaster ever coming to
pass.
• * •
Crick, M.L.A., the Tshmael of the Assem-
bly, now proposes to put these individuals to
the proof by moving for the immediate
abolition of the honorarium. He will be
supported by most of the old-established
Parliamentary robbers who find it more
profitable to pay themselves than be paid.
He Avill also be supported by sundry mem-
bers who don’t know how to pay themselves,
and whose very food and raiment depend on
their salaries, but who will join him to save
their credit with their constituents and in the
hope that the proposal will be lost, despite
their reluctant support. And it is just pos-
sible that too many of this latter class will
try to save their reputations for consistency
by voting against what they fondly trust is
a foregone conclusion, and may carry their
point against their will and to their own
destruction. Then the Council will gladly and
enthusiastically endorse their action, and the
good old days will come back, when none but
capitalists and thieves were qualified for
seats in the Assembly. The Labour-party is
the only one which can be relied upon to
give a solid vote on behalf of the present
system. Its members hold no portfolios,
get no fees as members of the Public Works
Committee, and have no present prospect of
any salaried billets from either party ; also,
they have no private estates to be improved
at the public expense, and, being watched by
both old parties, and by the capitalistic press,
and the L.E.L., have practically no chance
of “living on the game” in any underhand
sense. To them payment is a necessity, so
long as man requires food and is not allowed
to go round in a state of complete undress,
and they frankly admit the fact. Those
other members who do not admit the fact
either live on Ministerial salaries, or else are
wealthy capitalists who go into Parliament
mainly to see that the burden of taxation is
not shifted on to their own class, or else who
make their board and clothes out of their
Parliamentary positions in ways which won’t
bear inspection.
# * #
The one indubitable fact that stares the
community in the face is that no man works
gratis. Nobody ever offers to be a civil
servant for nothing, or to be a clerk for
nothing, or to be an artisan, or a physician,
or a monarch, or a Cabinet Minister without
pay, or to dig, or herd cattle, or hump bales
and boxes on a wharf, without remuneration
in any shape. If he did he would be inevit-
ably set down as a lunatic or a disguised
thief. Yet when hordes of politicians—
mostly hard, money-making men who were
never known in all their previous lives to
sacrifice cash for sentiment, or do anything
for nothing—profess themselves ready and
anxious to give up their time to the
exacting toil of looking after the public
interests, and to do it honestly and for no
ulterior motive, and not only to have no
desire for remuneration, but to be anxious
to pay large sums in election expenses for
the privilege of doing it, a very large
section of the community is willing to
take them at their word. ! The circum-
stance is incredible beyond the power of
words, but it exists none the less. Under
the present policy the great majority of can-
didates are mercenary individuals actuated
by a desire for the salary alone. In this
respect they are like everybody else in all
this world ; everybody who wants a billet of
any kind is a mercenary individual looking
after the salary. And, having got his billet,
the candidate who lives on his salary does
his best to fulfil the wishes and to promote
the interests of his employers—the nation—
in order that he may keep his billet and his
salary, in which respect he is like every other
servant, from a Permanent Under-Secretary
down to the cook in a sixpenny restaurant.
The stereotyped complaint that paid poli-
ticians are low and degraded because they
work mainly for their wages is just as absurd
as the complaint that labourers are mer-
cenary because they don’t dig the ground
gratis and from purely patriotic motives. If
the world can’t find monarchs, Cabinet
Ministers, clergymen, physicians, lawyers,
hangmen, and editors to toil without pay, it
need not expect to find politicians to do so
either. Also, it would be a lamentable thing
if it did find them. The man who offers to
take care of the national assets without
salary is just about 200 times more to be
suspected than the man who offers to manage
a bank gratis and to pay for the privilege of
doing so. The idea of a Parliament of pure,
disinterested and able patriots, serving for
the mere sake of patriotism, is magnificent
in theory ; but it is only theory. Such men
exist in all ages and in all countries, but no
small community ever grows or ever did
grow or ever will grow 125 of them at once.
Also, most of them have to toil for their
daily bread and cannot give up their lives to
legislation ; and even if they did exist and
were all men of independent means their
chance of being discovered and sent into the
Legislature is infinitesimal. The country
only finds out the true and disinterested
patriot when he advertises himself, and the
fact of his advertising is sufficient evidence
that he is no patriot, after all.
State Life Assurance.
Every now and then some audacious M.P.
dares to raise his impious voice in one of the
Australian legislatures to call attention to the
necessity that exists for regulating the business
of life assurance. Little good ever comes of
these spasmodic attempts to deal with a vital
question, and the cry of the complainer is
speedily drowned by the protests of the smug
crowd who have captured the directorates of
life-companies, against State interference with
this, the most sacred of all forms of “ private
enterprise.” The touchiness of the bank-man
is proverbial ; his brother, the life-insurance
director or manager, is, if possible, still more
touchy. But time teaches even Australians.
Recent events have convinced them —and they
needed to be convinced—that the proud fabric
of banking business is reared on a frail
foundation. That the same conviction has not
come home to them, in regard to companies
doing life business, arises from the circumstance
that the contingencies covered by assurance
are, for the most part, remote, not being likely
to arise for twenty-five or thirty years as an
average. If a hundred men of 30 years of age
insure to-day, not 33 claims will arise in 30
years, so that an office has always “a good
show ” before its delinquencies are found out
by anyone other than an expert. There is also
in favour of the insurance offices, a conspiracy
of silence on the part of the financial press. In
any other country, the methods pursued by
certain blatant offices doing business in these
provinces would have met with unqualified
condemnation. But here the faint murmurs of
disapproval, occasionally expressed in the
specialist papers, are hushed to silence on the
display of a sufficiently large advt.
• # •
It flatters our pride to think that, so far, no
office trading in Australia has failed, but, after
all, this is a small mercy to be thankful for, as
the average age of local offices is well under 20
years. All they crave is a little more time.
But if Australia yields no evidence of the
failure of a life company, England, the home of
all financial righteousness, offers a tempting
list to choose from. In 1844 the Joint Stock
Companies Act was passed. As if by magic
there sprang into existence, in the very next
year, a multitude of life-companies, of which 48
were registered provisionally, but only 20 com-
pleted their registration. At the end of 21
years only one of these companies remained
alive ; all the rest had died an unnatural death,
become insolvent, or been absorbed by other
companies. Taking 25 years as little enough
time to prove a life office, the test applied to
English offices is very unsatisfactory. Some
257 offices commenced business from 1845 to
1866, and of these not 20 are now existing—237
and more have gone under. This bare state-
ment of how they do things in England disposes
of the contention that life companies should
not be made the subject of State interference.
If making provision for the widow and the
orphan be the sacred and solemn duty that
popular opinion supposes it to be, then the
State has a solemn call to see that the money in-
tended for their use reaches them, and is not
diverted to the pockets of adventurers.
* • •
The principle of life-assurance is that for a
certain present payment or series of payments
a sum of money will be paid on a certain con-
tingency happening. That contingency is
usually the death of the person insuring. For
the yearly payment of about £1 17s. 7d. any
office will assure to a man now 30 years of age
the sum of .£IOO when he dies. This £1 17s. 73.
is made up of two parts, the actual risk, valued
at about £1 13s. 4d. and called the pure pre-
mium, and 4s. 3d., the loading, a provision for
expenses. Were all insurers 30 years of age it
would be permissible for the company insuring
to spend the 4s, 3d.— i.e., something less than
12 per cent, of the total premiums—in getting
or keeping their risks. Now, how does the
business of life-assurance square with these
facts ? The cost of working this class of busi-
ness in Australia ranges from 13 per cent, of
the premium income to over 31 per cent. —not
including industrial business, which runs up to
80 per cent.—that is to say, the most economi-
cally managed company conducts its business
at a price somewhat over the loading, while
the most expensive absorbs all the loading, and
besides, a lot of the money that should be set
apart to meet the risk when it happens.
» • •
It must not be hurriedly assumed from this
that all the companies are insolvent. While
business is being extended it is allowable for a
company to anticipate some of the loading of
future years to pay for present necessities, but
no sane man will contend that any society can
safely carry on which habitually pays away 30
per "cent, of its premium income to maintain
and extend its business, when the provision
made for such purposes is only 12 per cent.
Yet this is what several companies have been
doing ever since they started. Every decent
company makes its valuations on a pure
premium basis—that is to say, when making up
its assets it assumes that to cash in hand there
is added the present value of future payments
made by policy holders, on the assumption that
all the loading is required to work the business
and only the pure premiums are available for
risks. How then do societies that make
heavy inroads into their future resources come
out triumphantly under valuation ? Easily
enough. They can do as their banking friends
have done —write up their assets. By writing
up its landed property before realisation to the
tune of £24,000, one company was able to show
a surplus of £4200. This was a few years
ago, but how would such valuation stand to-
day ? Another way is to assume more than the
pure premium to be available to meet the risk,
which also is a deception. But why complete
the list? The ways open to an insurance man
bent upon bolstering up his company are
legion. Actuaries may be honest, as they some-
times are, and bring out the right valuation
from their point of view, but the other side of
the office —the business manager and directors
—can always adjust the value of the properties
to suit the exigencies of their position. As
often as not the actuaries are the mere tools of
the office that employs them : they make their
valuation to order. One company, not so long
ago, had a valuation made on a 5 per cent,
basis, which proved to be a very necessary pre-
caution, seeing that by this means the assets
were made to cover the liabilities.
• • «
All companies doing business here, including
foreign companies, reckon the interest obtain-
able during "the currency of their policies will
average 4 per cent., and their valuations are
made on the supposition that the money
received will be continuously invested at this
rate, the assumption being that over 4 per cent,
will be earned during the first half of the
period and less than that rate during the
remainder of the term covered by the policies.
Not much actuarial or other sort of knowledge
is needed to see that what is now earned over
4 per cent, should be set wholly or in part
against the less amount expected to be ob-
tained hereafter. Yet these so-called prudent
and conservative societies are coolly dividing
the present excess, instead of stowing it away
to meet the fall in interest which they them
selves assume to be inevitable. If the actuaries


did not break this elementary law of their
science their wretched companies would not
able to declare a bonus of any kind,
aa more than the margin out of which
bonuses are supposed to be paid is swal-
lowed up in maintaining an army ot touts.
p u t worse than this, there is a doubt even
about the 4 per cent., for no one outside an
assurance office will seriously contend there is
‘ V probability that safe investments will be
Procurable, ten years hence, for large amounts,
at so high a rate. Convinced of this the largest
local company is dropping its valuations
down to 3i per cent., and on a percent
basis how many companies in Australia would
be solvent ? When it is remembered that on
this basis the average premiums only allow for
an expenditure of per cent, for working
expenses, it will take a lot of figuring out to
prove that expenditure at the rate of 30 per
cent, can long be borne. As a matter of cal-
culation, it can be shown that in seven years
the whole provision for expenses will become
exhausted, and unless a constant influx of mem-
bers is maintained bankruptcy will be the
result. How then do societies keep afloat?
Simply by having two classes of members —
those who participate in the profits and those
who do not, the one class being charged a high
premium to admit of profits with economical
management, while the other is charged bare
expenses. As a matter of fact, few belong to
the second category, and societies are able to
carry on simply because what members fondly
suppose to be available for profits, is spent in
working the concern. We have little hope of
making our meaning clear by abstract reason-
ing, we therefore throw in an example from
their own returns which, doubtless, will be
pleasing to the societies selected—viz., three
whose head offices are in Melbourne. The com-
panies have all been in existence 20 years, and
have, therefore, got over the critical period of
babyhood # #
The annual revenue of the three offices from
premiums is ,£585,000, and of this, according to
their own showing, £457,000 is the value of the
risks they run, and £44,000 is required for
ordinary expenses, so that the sum available
for profits distributable as bonuses in every year
should be £84,000. The actual realisation is
somewhat different. As the result of 20 years’
work, these companies have given their mem-
bers bonuses equivalent to about £120,000 cash ;
that is to say, a year and a-halfs savings if
their management had not been extravagant.
All this points to what has long been well
known —competition has reached so acute a
stage as to endanger the very safety of the
competing offices. Amongst the ignorant mem-
bers of the Ancient Order of Cemented Bucks,
who are struggling for supremacy with the
Ultra-Marine Lodge of Holy Romans, such un-
healthy business might be expected. For them
the light of actuarial science shines in vain, but,
then, the friendly society people never pretend
to have any respect for science—their guide is
rule of thumb. With life companies it is
different. Their common cant is about ex-
pectation of life, mean-after-lifetime, gradation
of risks, and such like, and if they go to perdi-
tion it is with their eyes open wide. Assurance
offices one and all deprecate the cut-throat busi-
ness they are engaged in, but the sincerity of
their professions in this regard can be tested by
their actual performances. Passing over the
late disturbed years, and going back to 1892, it
will be found that the proportion of manage-
ment expenses to premium income was over
30 per cent, in two offices, from 25 to 30 per-
cent. in five, and from 20 to 25 in three, one
office standing solitary with less than 14 per
cent. As for the so-called industrial or working
men’s societies, they spend 13 to 16 shillings
out of every pound paid, and therefore should
be doing splendidly—for those who run them.
# * *
The evil is big, and admits of either of two
remedies, State regulation —which offices, one
and all, protest against as a tyrannous inven-
tion of the devil, and which, moreover, they
declare has not been successful in America,
where it is in operation —or State insurance.
In 1870, Julius Vogel introduced State life
assurance into Maoriland, and, after 20 years
of trial, no one disputes its signal success, for
no British office, established during the last 40
years has achieved equal results. The Maori-
' land State department charges lower rates than
any competing company, and, in spite ol this,
has accumulated more funds than any Aus-
tralian office, the A.M.P. Society, of course,
excepted. Maoriland’s example has not yet
been followed by any other province, though a
serious proposal for State insurance now comes
from South Australia. Against this proposal the
companies are up in arms. So far, their chief
argument against the proposal is that the State
must not move because the companies at work
_ have vested interests that should not be dis-
turbed. Quite true, and so (to use an extreme
illustration) Matthias Larkin felt as regards
his post when the shareholders tried to remove
him from his managership. But the broad
principles of public policy which justify the
State undertaking the business of life-assurance
are not likely to be affected by the cry of
vested interests. Above all things else it is the
duty of the State to look to the welfare of the
weaker members of the community. Hitherto,
its accepted duty has been to feed the fat and
support the strong. Public sentiment applauds
the man who makes provision for the well-being
of those who are bound to him by ties of rela-
tionship, and the same applause cannot be
denied to the State when it does the same for
those bound to it by ties of citizenship.
• • •
The insurance-companies cry out that the
provision is already secure through them. From
the references already made to insurance fin-
ance, it is evident that the security is at best
doubtful. The break-down of the insurance
companies would be a ten thousand times more
awful calamity than that of the banks, and who
can say that it will not happen ? The State alone
can give insurance which really insures. Even
the best of the companies is liable to be upset
by bad investments. Not so the State, since it
has all the security a private company can offer
with the public guarantee super-added. The
security is everything. What certainty is
there in regard to private offices ? Absolutely
none, and that the majority of them think so
may be gathered from the fact that, with one
or two bright exceptions, their published
balance-sheets and statements of business are
models of reticence. They are so judiciously
vague as to engender the thought that where
so much is concealed, there is a reason
for concealment more satisfactory to the
officials of the companies than reassur-
ing to their clients. The State has every
reason to be distrustful, and where so much is
at stake it does well to take care no risks are
run. To certainty of insurance the State is in
a position to join cheapness. Its officers are in
every part of the country, their agency will cost
little, and the quarter of a million yearly ex-
pended on insurance bagmen will be saved to
the policy-holder, as well as the larger part of
a like amount laid out on office management.
State life assurance is, however, only one step
in the right direction. There is no reason in
the world why the State should not undertake
the duty now grievously bungled by the friendly
societies, and provide relief in sickness and
annuities in old age to all who choose to avail
themselves of its good offices.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Inconvertible Paper Currency.
The weary debate on the Victorian Credit
Fonder Bill was interrupted lately by a sug-
gestion for getting the province, to some extent
at least, out of the old rut of finance. Under
the Bill it is proposed to borrow £5,000,000 at
4 per cent, and lend it out at 5 per cent, on
laud-mortgages with a 50 per cent, margin. A
revolutionary M.L.A. interrupted the solemnity
of the proceedings just here by suggesting that,
instead of borrowing the £5,000,000 and lend-
ing it at 5 per cent., the State should issue
£5,000,000 in legal tender notes, and lend them
out on mortgage at 4 per cent. This would
bring in £200,000 a year in interest, and each
year the interest would be used to redeem
£200,000 worth of notes until the whole issue
was extinguished. When it was entirely ex-
tinguished the State would have £5,000,000
invested on land-mortgages and an annual
income of £200,000, which had cost it abso-
lutely nothing, and it was suggested that this
income should be “ ear-marked,” according to
the good old Gillies phrase, as a sinking-fund
for the reduction of the public debt. If the
demand for loans increased, the Govern-
ment notes, as they returned to the Land
Bank in the shape of interest, might be rein-
vested in new mortgages, but the total amount
to be circulated at any time should never exceed
£5,000,000. This was the astounding pro-
posal which flabbergasted the stereotyped
borrowers of the Assembly, and after a few
minutes of horrified consideration it was
thrown out almost unanimously. The Vic.
Legislature has no inclination for “ wild
experiments” or “ reckless legislation” in any
shape ; it is a gathering of eminently serious
spirit-dealers, lawyers and land-boomers, and
anything revolutionary inspires it with horror.
Yet the proposition deserved a lot of hard con-
sideration, which it certainly didn’t get. The
question to be decided is—could Victoria carry,
for a limited period, £5,000,000 of inconvertible
paper money, without depreciation ? In all
probability it could. Similar experiments have
succeeded elsewhere, and their success depends
solely on honest management. The circulation
of such an amount in State-guaranteed notes
would mean the extinction of the note issues of
private banks, but that is a matter of exceed-
ingly small importance, and would do more
good than harm. If the experiment were
carried out honestly, and succeeded, it would
mean an immense gain to the community ; and
if it failed it couldn’t, by any human possibility,
fail so badly as the good, old borrowing policy
has done and is still doing. The constantly-
reiterated objection to an inconvertible paper
currency, even as a small and temporary ex-
pedient, is that there is always a tendency to go
on expanding it until a ruinous depreciation sets
in. But the Same is equally true of any other
device for borrowing, either openly or in disr
guise. Victoria has raised loans in the English
market until she is wrecked and nearly insol-
vent; and when the choice lies between more
pubhc borrowing and the issue of a limited and
temporarily .inconvertible State currency for
the of the Land Bank only, the
odds lobk very much in favour of the latter
device. Anyhow, Victoria is just now a
favourable field in which to try an experiment
of the kind, for all the ancient methods of finance
are played out. And the one thing that is
certain about the new idea is that, even at its
worst, it couldn’t be worse than the old ones.
How Things Happen.
“ The king is dead ; long live the king !” The
Griffilwraith is also dead ; but Boodlewraith
has succeeded it. Boodlewraith, to be sure,
has no flesh and blood individuality whatever,
being merely The Bulletin’s pet name for an
imaginary little cherub who sits up aloft to
guide the destinies of Queensland. But its
euphonic merit, and its ability to dispense with
the definite article, are no misleading signs of
superiority. The vigour of the Griffilwraith
was slightly impaired by an extraordinary
disease which one of the principals called his
“ principles.” Now, foxes have holes, but the
“principles” of the Griffilwraith, well mixed
with baser matter, found nowhere to lay their
heads and sub-heads among the cloven feet
which that strange pair of bedfellows protruded.
So they expired in a ruinous Samsonian spasm,
which pulled down a fee of £2778 3s. hd. and a
salary of £3500 per annum for the fortunate
proprietor —together with an eloquent eulogy
from a junior patriot (fee, £2968 135.), who
vainly ransacked history for heroes pure enough
to hang their hats upon the golden knobs of his
client’s characteristic character. The trap closed
finally on the Griffilwraith, and, with a
sulphurous smell of overdrafts, Boodlewraith
sprang up. Then things began to hum. Pre-
viously things had happened. So early as
1878, when the Union Bank of Australia held
the Queensland Government’s account, and the
funds at fixed deposit were pretty equally
divided among the banks doing business in
the province, it occurred to E. R. Drury,
manager of the Queensland National Bank,
that it would be advantageous to the public if
further droppings fell upon him. He pressed
this view upon J. R. Dickson, Treasurer of
the Douglas Ministry, in an interesting corres-
pondence, with the result that Mr. Dickson
felt himself profoundly grieved to be unable to
see eye to eye with Mr. Drury. If money did
not have such a short memory, it would seem
not improbable that Drury kept all Dickson’s
sayings in his heart; for, at the general elec-
tion of 1879, the whole of the Q.N. Bank in-
fluence was cast against the Douglas Ministry,
and Mr. Thomas MTlwraith became Premier
of the new Government on January 21. On
April 26 the Government agreed to transfer its
account to the Q.N. Bank, but the Union in-
sisted on six months’ notice. So things couldn’t
happen much till September. On Sept. 10,
1879, the Q.N. Bank held £270,000 of Govern-
ment money. On Sept. 11 it held £1,303,134.
That was the beginning of the Q.N. Bank
Government moneyopoly. Since that date
Queensland has borrowed, in round numbers,
£25,000,000, nearly the whole of which has
passed through the Q.N. Bank and paid toll in
the shape of half-yearly dividends. It is only
the first step which costs, and once things
commenced to happen they kept on. MTl-
wraith left office in 1883, but by that
time the Bank was firmly entrenched. MTl-
wraith came back to office in 1888 and floated
a million loan. Then the little sum stood :
June 30, 1889— Govt, funds in Q.N. Bank £2,308,418
„ ~ ~ ~ Other Banks 1,000.000
In 1889, MTlwraith left the Morehead
Ministry, but returned to power as Griffilwraith
Treasurer on August 12, 1890. Then things
no longer happened ; they hurried. See :
June 30, 1891—Govt, funds in Q.N. Bank £1,286,459
~ „ ~ ~ Other Banks 450,000
June 30, 1892 ~ ~ Q.N. Bank 1,903,793
„ ~ ~ ~ Other Banks 265,000
But March 27, 1893, saw the dissolution of the
Griffilwraith, and then things fairly hummed.
Look :

~ ~ ~ ~ 6 other Banks 486,000
Then the 8.N.5.W., the Australasia, and the
Union dropped out of the running, so that we
have;—
June 30, 1893—Govt, funds in Q.N. Bank £3,000,000
~ ~ ~ ~ 3 other banks 181,000
Just here it seems appropriate to remark that
(1) No one has any idea what the Q.N. Bank
owes—if it owes anything—to the MTlwraith
party. (2) No one has any idea what the MTl-
wraith party owes — if it owes anything — to
the Q.N. Bank. These are axioms. Axioms
are self-evident propositions. Propositions sup-
port theories. Sometimes theories don’t alto-
gether explain how things happen. Sometimes
they do.
“The Natural Rate of Interest.”
The Maoriland Government proposes to start a
Credit Fonder of its own, and, with this object
in view, it intends to borrow £1,500,000 at 3J
per cent. —whether in London or within its own
province is not yet known with certainty, and
lend it out at 5 per cent, on mortgages of agri-
cultural lands, in order to relieve the farmer
from the usury with which he is now burdened.
The borrowing at 3| per cent, is a lamentable
device for a province which has abjured kite-
flying for several years, and was believed to
have abandoned it for ever, and if the Seddon
Cabinet had only a little more courage it might
have provided the required capital by an issue
of State notes, its credit being fully good
enough for such an experiment. As to the
lending part of the scheme, it will, if honestly
and capably conducted, be an unmingled bless-
ing to a community which has hitherto been
exploited by financial marauders of the ten per
sheut persuasion. Up to date, the loudest voice
which has been raised in execration of the
scheme has been that of the Sydney Telegraph,
which evidently fears that the Credit Fonder
idea may spread to New South Wales, and
holds forth on sound Foreign-trade principles as
follows :
The cry is that unless the State comes to the farmfrs’
assistance with cheap mortgages and cheap railway
freights the whole of the lout:-coddled agricultural in-
dustry stands in danger of perishing. It is impossible to
view any of these schemes without feelings of the very
gravest apprehension. For the State to attempt to arti-
ficially cheapen money is just the same as to attempt
artificially raising the price of products. In each case the
whole difference between the natural and the artificial
rate must come out of the pockets of the taxpayers. If
the fanners get cheap money from a State Bank or big
prices in a State-protected market, it can only be at the
expense of the rest of the community.
The fact that the Credit Fonder scheme has
been an unalloyed success for many years in
many countries evidently counts for nothing.
The State Bank is coming near, and the Tele-
graph, which admired a State Bank as long as
there seemed to be no danger of getting one,
grows alarmed. As for fits reasoning, that is
drivel of the most abject order. The State
doesn’t propose to artificially cheapen money —
it simply proposes to cheapen money ; there is
nothing artificial about cheap money, and no-
thing especially natural about dear money. The
assertion that “the whole difference between
the natural and the artificial rate must come
out of the pockets of the taxpayers,” has a
sonorous sound something like that of a gong in
the distance, but it means nothing. The money
which is now lent to the farmer at 8 or 10 per
cent, is mostly British capital; the money which
will be lent by the State at 5 per cent, will also,
unfortunately, be mostly British capital; there-
fore, the difference will come out of the pockets
of the money-lender, and his alone. Provided
the Credit Fonder is a success the only loser
will be the money-lender. Moreover there is
no “ natural ” or “ artificial ” rate of interest ;
the rate of interest, both natural and artificial,
is simply as much as the lender can get, and
whether he gets per cent, or 240 makes no
difference. When the British Government sells
Consols at 2| per cent, per annum, and when
some gaudy ass borrows from Cohen at 20 per
cent, per month, the processes are equally
natural. Also, when a reduction is made in
either case it is equally “at the expense of the
community ” —always presuming that Cohen is
the community, which he isn’t. The Tele-
graph, however, evidently takes it for granted
that he is.
A Person of no Account*
Last Thursday, at Sydney, one John Camp*
bell was arraigned for the murder of his wife.
The prisoner was a man without means (or
perhaps, having means, he was not sane enough
to call for and instruct a lawyer), of no
social standing, and with no rich or influential
friends, and the proceedings took the form
which is usual under such circumstances. The
prosecution was represented by Mr. Barton,
ex-Attorney-General, ex-Speaker of the Legis-
lative Assembly, a Queen’s Counsel, and one of
the very ablest men at the New South Wales
bar. His case was carefully prepared for him
by the Crown Law offices and the police and
detectives, who had got together all the facts
which could possibly assist the prisoner towards
the gallows, and had collected an array of
evidence to support the case for the “ Crown.”
And against all this there was absolutely
nothing ; every arrangement had been made to
hear one side of the case, and no arrangement
whatever to hear the other. Under these lop-
sided conditions the Judge adjourned for one
whole hour —60 minutes—and at the end of that
time a very junior barrister almost an
infant barrister, indeed turned up to
conduct the defence. He had no solicitor
to prepare his case, or to hunt up witnesses ;
he had no time or money or opportunity to
master the facts of the case, or to consider the
evidence for the prosecution, or to get a coherent
statement for his client, or to procure any
medical opinions as to his state of mind, or to
discover the antecedents of the deceased, or to do
anything. In fact, he had no case at all, and even
if he had possessed such a thing he was pitted
against one of the best and most experienced
intellects at the local bar. Therefore, he
had practically to let matters slide and con-
tent himself with what was really only an
appeal for mercy on no visible grounds, and his
client was duly sentenced to be hanged, which
he, at least technically, deserved to be in any
case. But if he had been an innocent man
wrongly accused he would almost assuredly
have been sentenced just the same under the
circumstances. The system which pits a junior
barrister with barely an hour to prepare his
case, against the Treasury, one of the leaders of
the bar, the Crown Solicitor with his trained
staff of assistants, the detective and police force
and a host of expert witnesses, is murder,
pure and simple. A law-case is as intricate
a piece of mechanism as a watch, and when a
prisoner, who, being in gaol, has had no oppor-
tunity to prepare his own cause, and has had
no one to do it for him, is brought out and
ordered to produce a fully-elaborated defence
on the spot or be hanged for want of it, his
position is much the same as if he were pre-
sented with a mass of pig-iron, and ordered to
make a chronometer on the instant as the price
of his life. The whole system is radically
wrong. It is not the business of the law to act
as purveyor to the gallows. Its mission is not
to obtain convictions promiscuously but to find
out the truth, and it gains as much by the
acquittal of the man who is wrongly accused as
by the conviction of the one who is accused
rightly. Therefore it should furnish as perfect
and as elaborate machinery for the defence as it
does for the prosecution, and until it does this
it is only a lopsided device which gives justice
to the man who has money and “lags” the
man who has none, simply because he Has none.
Pear Bulletin, —The statement that the New
South Wales labour vote in 1894 was only half
what it was in 1891 is misleading. Everything
depends upon classification of candidates ; and
as there are no exact data upon which to class
them, there cannot possibly be an exact con-
clusion. Both Herald and Telegraph endea-
vour to swell the Freetrade vote at the expense
of labour, but even accepting the worst classifi-
cation, that of the Telegraph, which counts
Cook and other unpledged Labour representa-
tives as Freetraders, it will be seen that the
Protectionist vote in 1891 was reduced by 48
per cent., the Labour vote by 52 per cent., and
the Freetrade vote by no less than 58 per cent.
Here are the Telegraph’s own figures. In
1891 : Freetrade, 177,769; Protection, 133,565 ;
Labour, 103,787 ; Independent, 10,997. This
last group consisted of Haynes, Jones, REiD t
Want, and J. P. Abbott, all of whom, wit
the exception of Abbott, who was returned uuh
opposed, were Freetraders. Add them on and-
we have:—
Freetrade. Protection. Labour.
1891 188,766 133,565 103,787
1894 78,994 68,470 48,980
Reduction 109,772 65,095 54,807
Personally I put little reliance on the figures
for 1891, nor can I agree with either of the
quoted aggregates for Labour, which are
obtained by a manifestly unfair classification
of members. But, accepting the Telegraph
figures, no comment is more eloquent than the
mere statement of the respective reductions of
the three parties ; Labour, 54,807 ! Protection,
65,095 ! ! Freetrade, 109,772 ! ! !—Yours, &c.,

The Wild Cat Column.
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney shows
a net profit for the half-year of £25,600, and pays a div.
at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum, which absorbs
£33,900, so the co. ends £BOOO worse off than it
began. For a vast concern like the Commercial, with
£886,000 of paid-up capital, and £1,010,000 of reserve
fund, and over £3,000,000 of cash on hand, this is a matter
of little importance, but to some of the recon-
structed institutions it would be a serious affair. The
apparent falling off in the profits is chiefly owing to the
large sums which have been set aside to meet possible
depreciation. Meanwhile, the Commercial Banking Co.
occupies a curious position. It lately offered, informally,
to release a large proportion of its reconstructed deposits,
but as these are now selling at par. in the open market,
and as there is no present prospect of getting the
same interest elsewhere with as good security,
the owners almost unanimously declined the offer. The
reconstructed depositors in Mr. T. A. Dibbs’ bank refuse
to take their money and leave unless they are violently
“chucked out.” As the bank has no present use for a
great part of the money it is now considering the advis-
ability of chucking them out accordingly. The situation
is weird and anomalous ; no other bank in Australasia has
ever succeeded in getting into such a position, though a
very large number would like to do so. If the Commercial
Banking Co.’s deposits could be guaranteed against re-
payment until the end of their term they would probably
fetch 21s. in the £ on Sydney Exchange.
• * * *
The .£2,000,000 of State-guaranteed 4 per cent,
stock issued by the Bank of New Zealand was tendered
for five time* over. It now probably dawns on the stupid
individual who planned the scheme that the cash could
have been obtained about as easily at 3£, and probably, if
the stock had been sold openly at a fixed price instead of
being disposed of by tender, it might have realised almost
a* good a figure. The Maoriland Government can borrow
money with ease on its own aecurity at 3J per cent., and
in the present case there is the security of the Govern-
ment and that of the 8.N.Z., whatever the latter may be
worth, thrown in.
• * • ♦
Mr. Wynne, Solicitor-General in the Patterson
Government, has a measure before the House which pro-
vides, among other things, that trading companies may
work under the no-liability principle. The effect of this will
simply be to abolish that delusive asset, “ uncalled
capital.” On the no-liability principle calls are legally
irrecoverable; the shareholder who won’t pay simply
forfeits his shares. Under such a law no insolvent trading
00. or bank could collect apenny in calls, for no one would
pay in such circumstances, and a good many of the banks
which are still alive would be in much the same situation,
for nobody voluntarily pays a ss. call on shares which
can’t be »old for half-a-crown.
The change is a move in the right direction,
anyhow. “Uncalled capital,” as a rule, only serves to
delude the public with a bogus aspect of solidity, and it is
only realisable when it isn’t wanted. An enormous pro-
portion of the deposits gathered in by the Mercantile
“ tentacle*” were secured because the uncalled capital in
each case was so massive that it seemed to promise abso-
lute security, no matter what happened. And when the
trouble came, the security in every case proved to be
mainly Matthew Davies, and Matthew, in the face of this
stupendous mass of liabilities, wasn’t worth anything.
Most other people, under like conditions, are worth just
about as much. “ Uncalled capital ” is as beautiful as a
rainbow and about as good a security to advance money
on. Anybody who has ever had a lien upon a rainbow
will understand this.
« « • «
This is a woefully bad time for the man who
has borrowed money on excellent security from a recon-
structed bank. Also.it is a conespondingly good time
for the man who has borrowed huge sums on very bad
security, or on no security at all. The bank has to make
a show of accumulating a large cash balance because
there is a mania for disproportionate cash balances, now
that the depositors have all been “ fixed ” and there is no
immediate demand tor cash. And, in addition to this,
it is necessary to inform the shareholders at each
half-yearly meeting that the liquidation of the “old
bank ” is progressing satisfactorily, and that a
large sum has been realised, and that the assets
are turning out well. And the only way to do
this is to sell up only those customers whose
advances are sure to turn out well. If they sold up
instead the old, hopeless boomers who pay neither inter-
est nor principal, and will never pay either, the re-
port would probably state that £1,000,000 worth of
ifsets had been realised, and had produced about
£50,000. Then there would be a howl of fury and a panic.
So certain of the banks keep selling up and bankrupting
the honest men, who pay interest on their overdrafts
regularly, and whose securities are worth three times the
amount advanced on them ; and very often they lend the
money thus realised to the utterly hopeless boomers, lest
they should go insolvent, and the amount of their over-
drafts should he revealed on their schedules, and horrid
revelations should ensue. It is a strange world.
* • * *
The A. J.S. Bank has, during the last year, paid
off £972,320 of its indi-btedness to reconstructed deposi-
tors, and has advanced over £1,000,000 on the security of
deposit-receipts. It has £241,000 invested in Govt, bonds,
*nd it has a million and a quarter in available cash, which
Is actually more than all its liabilities on current accounts,
bills, notes and balances due to other banks. This
illustrates the state of excessive caution which the big re-
constructed banks have got into. The A.J.S. could safely
invest £500,000 more in Government debentures and thus
make over £16,000 per annum in interest for its share-
holders, or it could pay off £500,000 of its old fixed
deposits ; but last year’s disaster has created a mania for
stupendous cash-balances, at least halt of which are only
wanted for the board to gloat over.

The farce of a Melb. Insolvency Court examina-
tion into the affairs of H. W. Farrar and Co. has been
duly played—to what purpose? The most Inexplicable
feature of the liabilities, viz., the firm’s debt to the
Commercial Bank, was seemingly passed over, and the
enquiry closed in a haze of mystery as to a certain
judge’s real connection with the business. There is
always a mysterious termination to any scandal that
touches a member of the legal brotherhood.

Melbourne daily paper for months past. There was never

The Commercial Bank of Australia’s preference
shares (£lO paid up) could be had last week for 40s. These
shares were created out of reconstructed deposits, and
consequently represent, at above prices, a loss of over
80 per cent, to the miserable depositor.
• * * •
In Melb. there are yet many hundreds under
the thumbs of the building societies, dribbling away pay-
ments, which are to tot up to four times the value of the
properties. To be sure, a multitude have forfeited with
all their hopes gone. Others hang on, not liking to let
go the last rag, and with a forlorn hope that gas is to
come from somewhere to inflate all the empty bladders
hanging about on the angry sea. But the entrepreneur
is pricked and his bailoony stomach flaps in the wind,
like a ragged sail on the mainmast of a coffin barque.
• • ♦ *
The reconstructed Bank of North Queens-
land announces that it will at once set free all
fixed deposits under £5O, and 25 per cent, of ail
its other fixed deposits. Another 25 per cent, will
be set free in a month or two. The first instalment
isn’t due, under the terms rf the arrangement, till 1896,
but before that time the B.N.Q. hopes, with reasonable
good fortune, to liberate all its reconstructed funds. Also,
it has decided to take no more British deposits for less
than five years—the short-dated British investor having
been the cause of its previous troubles. Wanted to know
—does the Q.N.B. feel like following suit in this liberal
policy ?
• * * •
Melbourne Tramway Go. did the moat astonish-
ing act of its career last Saturday, when it declared a 5
percent, div. The actual profits for the year are only
£19,723, and the div. absorbs £24,000, but the deficiency
is made up out of the last year’s balance. Even the fact
that 4 per cent, has actually been earned is something
calculated to make the shareholder fall down dead with
surprise. The result has been arrived at mainly through
hard economy, and bv consistently grinding the hones of
the miserable ; and there are grave hope* that
by a little further grinding the present div. may be
maintained.

• • • •
A recent arrival from Westralia remarks to The
Bulletin that little has so far been said in the East con-
cerning one of the greatest difficulties of Westralian gold-
mining— i.e., the salt water, as it affects steam-boilers.
Most pitiful stories are told of the difficulties met with in
this direction. In some places, after working for a few
hours, a boiler has to be blown down and four inches of
salt scraped out of it. Repairs are difficult, and The
Bulletin informant saw many disused boilers out of
which the crowns had been fairly eaten.

60,4000 z.
* * * *
Somebody has been interviewing the Coolgardie
niggers, who say that on two previous occasions, “ long
time ago ” (17 years was the earliest), white men struck
the big quartz blow south of Bayley’s, and worked until
their water ran out, when some were speared and some
disappeared. Now the whites are back for the third time;
and this time they have come to stay.

There were “Bayleys” and “ Londonderrya ”
in bygone days. Since the discovery of gold on the
Thames (Maoriland) £6,600,000 worth has been won and
treated at the works on the field in 27 years. The most
famous finds were the Shotover and the Caledonian,
which in four months turned out £950.000. The Cale-
donian, perhaps the biggest show of reef-gold ever seen
in the world, unearthed £750,000 in one-third of a year,
* • « »
No silver-lead mine in the world approaches in
prosperity the Broken Hill Proprietary mine, with the
metals at present prices.
Proprietary half-year’s work represents 257,278
tons of ore treated, producing 7,287,3370 z. silver, 25,638
tons lead, and 441 tons copper—weekly average 280,2820 z.
silver, 986 tons lead, and 17 tons copper. At the refinery
at Port Pirie 10,117 tons of silver-lead bullion were
treated, result being 9108 tong lead, 3,037,8380 z. fine
silver, 13410 z. gold, 165 tons antimonial metal, and 330
tons copper matte, representing 158 tons copper and
45,1750 z. silver. About two-fifths of the bullion was thus
refined ; the rest exported. Ultimately the co. will refine
all its products and treat the copper matte which is now
sold abroad at a disadvantage.
Proprietary open cuts, the most daring and
original mining work in progress in Australia, now
extend practically the whole length of the mine ; depth,
from 30ft. to 100 ft. During past half-year they produced
78,159 tons ore, and 196,113 superficial feet of mine timber
were recovered. They have rendered yields erratic, but
mining economical, the manager says.
Some Proprietary figures Value of silver,
lead, copper, and gold produced (nine years), £12,033,948;
general expenditure (including depreciation), £6,393,285 ;
paid to shareholders, £7,296,000 (cash, £6,652,000; no-
minal value of shares in Block 14, British, and Block 10

Central (Broken Hill) prospects continue to
improve. Resumption of smelting is not a remote con-

The two deep finds at Gympie—the Great
Monkland reef cut at 1475 feet, and the 3 North Phoenix
at 1042 feet—both came on the same day. The Monkland
reef shows fine gold ; and gold is expected in the Phoenix
when it cuts the slate overhead. If payable ore is got,
the field will turn another page of prosperity.

Wyalong is red with the ructions of rival store-
keepers, who want the Post-office planted agin their own
door. Wyalong proper squats on an unhealthy flat, and
the Government have very wisely put the main township
on an elevated site a couple of miles away, which is fairly
central now that the field is so much extended. And the
ancient squatters naturally howl. Recent crushings—
Perry’s, 27 tons, 105 oz.; Currajong, 67 tons, av., 2oz.
12dwt.; Clements’, 45 tons, av., loz. 13dwt. ; Ormsby’s,
24 tons, av., nearly 2oz. Total crushings at Barmedman
battery to date are 1234 tons for 20490 z., or loz. 13dwt.
sgr. per ton.

Latest accounts say there are many diggers on
the Sud Est(New Guinea)alluvial, some working hydrauli-
cally ; also, that the reef-prospectors have followed down
outcrops without coming upon anything rich. Which
hardly squares with the news to hand two months ago,
and the nice “Sud Est specimens” lately brought to
Sydney.

How many remember Mat. Larkin’s appeal to
the South *Melb. footballers to play the manly, honest
game? This was when Mat. was president of the club,
just prior to shifting his place of residence from South
to North. Mat’s words drew tears from the South boys’
eyes, and they vowed never to do it again.

At Poverty Point.
After two year*’ residence in the train of Melba, the
diva’s sisters, Misses Mitchell, found the quiet of their
Melbourne home more than a trifle trying. Papa David,
however, holds out against continental tours, and so his
daughters have deserted their home by the Yarra and
gone to Colbin-Abhin, one of his station residences, which
they consider the next best thing to Paris.
Duncan Macallum, Dan Barry’s manager, goe* Coolgar-
diewards with a variety show and a tent.
It is a fact that during the first ten “showing" days
that Pillis’ Circus was in Adelaide two matinees were
Stopped by the rain, and that during every other perform-
ance tho heavens poured down a perfect deluge on the
tent.
Charles Saunders is writing articles for an English
paper, and it was in that interest that he attended the
exhumation of the Needles. He is annoyed that the
papers should have made “copy” out of the incident.
In a crowd of a thousand, the substantial tenor would be
picked out as an arsenic-eater himself owing to the white
puffiness of his face, but he does not patronise the drug.
Flora Oraupner seems in robust health again, and no
one understands quite why she is not with the Comic
Opera Co. Her old teacher, Madame Simonsen, is again
in charge of Flora’s octaves.
Essie Jenyns is said to have just written a book.
Lincoln, whose drama, “The Bush King,” attheSurrey
Theatre, was described as a failure by The Bulletin's
London correspondent, has given up the struggle for a
playwright’s laurels in London, and is now en route for
his comfortable home in Melbourne. Meanwhile, Lestocq
has promised his friendly interest to future productions
from young Lincoln’s pen.

When the Holloway co. was in Bendigo some yean
ago a local newspaper’s account was sent to Mrs. Hollo-
way (Essie Jenyns’ mother)—she always was cashier-
made out as for “ Professor Holloway." The lady’s ire at
the blunder was awful. As she put it:—“The idea of
being confounded with a quack vendor of pills 1” The
newspaper manager mildly murmured something about
Holloway’s immense fortune, but Mrs. Plain Bill only
sighed.
Tenor Leumane, who had a financial crash in Adelaide
over his English opera season, is at present very ill.
However, he finds time to write the press pro-
mising full payment of his debts. Leumane has an
honourable record and should be given a good benefit,
especially as others would also be assisted thereby.
Talmage is very emotional in preaching. At Exetei
Hall, on one occasion, he was so moved to tears, that
when he leaned over the pulpit, the reporters had to put
up an umbrella to prevent their notes being obliterated.

The quarrel between the late Trebellini-Bettini and her
daughter, concerning which there have been cables to
Australia, began in a very Frenchy way. Trebelli and
her daughter went to Sweden in company with that
fascinating French fiddler, Musin. Before many days,
Musin declared his love for the daughter, then 16, who,
though reciprocating, said she must ask her mamma, as
all French girls do, they say. She accordingly told
mamma, who was horrified, and said, “It cannot be, he
already loves me 1 I will give you proofs of it.” An
appointment was made by her with Lothario, and the
daughter was placed outside a door whilst he made violent
love to mamma in the room. This was too much for the
girl, who from that day bade good-bye to her mother and
never spoke to her again.
At his last show to-night, Talmage will talk about God,
men and monkey*.
“ The Firm ” has resolved to produce Massenet’*
“ La Navarraise ” in Australia as quickly as possible.
This brief tragic opera at once sprang into a mighty suc-
cess when brought out, for the first time on any stage, in
London, a few weeks ago. It is obviously suggested by
“Cavalleria Rusticana” and “Pagliacci,” and is expected
to go round the continent in the same wildfire manner.
There are good parts for Miss Stewart and Brownlow.
G. Majeroni may date his career from “ Geo. Barnwell.”
He was the only man in the Co. who could look it.
Billoloway might tackle Romeo, butCbas. was too modest
for Barnwell.
Aug. W. Juncker’s pretty song, “ I was Dreaming,”
which Nellie Stewart has made such a big success, has, it
is stated, already run through a first edition of 8000
copies. The publisher, Mr. H. J. Samuell, has a second
edition ready.
Decima Moore, who, rumour says, comes here shortly,
is a singularly -piquant actress with a sweet and flexible
voice. She is one of four clever daughters of a Brighton
(Eng.) chemist.

can’t act no longer holds good. “In the mad-scene of
‘Lucia’ she introduced herself as a new creature, a
living, suffering woman, not a mere operatic figure with
some well-placed top notes.”

quote you, a few Sundays ago, as one of those with a
special ‘ grace of God,’ to awaken in hearts we parsons
cannot reach, appreciation of a faithful woman’s com-
radeship in the hard battle of life."

A young Australian lady, who has just made a brilliant
musical name in England, had a prodigiously narrow
escape on the voyage. Her mother angled to capture, for
her, a desirable party on board, but the girl’s success
reduces such a party to ineligibility. Thus an angel
watches aloft o’er poor Jill, as well as poor Jack.
The cyoloratna business is likely to have a big revival*
shortly, an apparatus having been patented which will
permit a weekly change of picture if desired.


From remarks made at Lambton by Mr.
Hughes, M.L.A., it would appear that when
Sydney Daily Post does come out, it will be as a
“ Democratic morning paper.” The machinery is
now being erected in a Pitt-st. building, two or
three doors from the site of the old Evening News
Office. There will be room for the Post —if the
editor is strong enough to slay every would-be
M.P. and debating-club amateur who wants to
write leading articles.
H. J. Samuell’s publications are now a
household word throughout Australia. His last
publication—Aug. W. Juncker’s lovely song,
“ I was Dreaming”—is now in its second edition,
three thousand copies having been sold. Miss
Nellie Stewart sang it for six weeks in Melbourne
in “ Ma Mie Rosette,” where it was encored every
evening. Mr. Samuell has in the press a charming
“ Slumber Song,” by Harrison White, which Miss
Colbourne Baber will introduce to the Sydney
public. “ The Duchess of York Waltz” is still
the favourite with dancers, being recommended
by Ernest Needs, the leading teacher of dancing
in Sydney. You can always get SarnueU’s
publications from W. H. Paling and Co., Ltd.,
of Sydney and Brisbane; or Allan and Co., and
W. H. Glen and Co., of Melbourne. If your local
musicsellers do not stock them, send a postal
order for two shillings each to Mr. H. J. Samuell,
direct to his box, 1077, G.P.0., Sydney, N.S.W.*
