Mt Dear Moorabinda—
Canine history merely repeated itself at the dog-
show at Sydney Exhibition last week, and I dis-
covered no new designs in puppies, and (as usual)
no attempt at artistic staging for the multitude of
bow-wows who got through on their personal
merits only. And yet, although the spectacular
aspect of the annual parade of doggies would be
infinitely improved if Toby were set in a frame of
art muslin instead of straw and chewed-up dog-
biscuit, I still must confess that the squalid sur-
roundings pleased me. I positively exulted in the
presence of an impish toy terrier roosting on a
folded-up undervest and a foggy Skye squatting
on a soiled and torn calico pillow. For these
doggies were obviously ladies’ pets, and it is good
to know that the Australian woman is not the bond-
slave of her pug, but will cheerfully chain up the
pampered beast in an empty gin-case without even
referring to the possibility of rusty nails. Though
the dogs corralled most of the attention, there was
a good flutter of feathers, from tom-tits to turkeys,
and it will ever be a matter of surprise to me why
no fat lady has yet come forth with a stuffed
Langshan cockerel in her bonnet, since his plumage
would make a bird of Paradise look sallow.
Lady Duff, in a dress the colour of cinnamon-
stick, trimmed with velvet and tuck-pointed with
gold cord, fur boa and muff, and the inevitable
toque hat en suite, opened the show, per Major
Leigh, whose yapping way of announcing the fact
fitted the occasion admirably. But although down
for a speaking-part, the only audible sound that
came from her Ladyship was when she gazed into
the vice-president’s face and said “Wot!” Potts
Point will please accept this intimation._ It is
evidently not Guv’mentousey to “ beg parding.”
The prettiest woman who went to the dogs
when I was there was a young English matron in
a black gown and a waist_ you could belt with a
garter, whose sister is a reigning London beauty,
I am told ; and the best form was displayed by a
Maoriland visitress in brown serge and furs and
an effective daffodil in her buttonhole, who talked
with a thick English accent, after the manner of
Maorilandresses, whose peculiarity is that they
can emerge from a place with a name like Whare-
ongaonga and talk fluent Picoadillyisms without
turning a hair. Mrs. “Uncle” Sutton turned
up with three hearse-plumes in her hat; but I was
really more interested in watching society’s heels
than its headpiece, since it was the fashion to hold
your skirts above high-buttoned-up-boots-raark as
you strolled about. It was a style that suited
some people, but I will never forget a stout society
lady in black, who hoisted her draperies to muddy-
road heights and presented the awful spectacle of
a fat woman careering down tLie hill of Time on a
pair of legs far too pipe-stemmy for their burden.
If I had a voice in dog-show arrangements, I
would certainly issue a ukase against visitors
touching the exhibits. _ Everybody pats Toby
when he wags his tail in a friendly way, and it
occurred to me as I saw a suspicious character
making flattering overtures to a fierce but im-
pressionable watch-dog, that, in a burgling age
like the present, the man who plants his house-
dog in a public show for Bill Sikes to make friends
with, isn’t acting in the best interests of his spoons
and forks.
I didn’t expect to unearth a V-shaped audience
at Dr. Talmage’s (I have heard him pronounced
Talmige, Talmahge and Talmidge, but he calls
himself Talmayge, if you please) first utterances
in Australia, at Sydney Centenary Hall on 26th
ult., so I wasn’t disappointed at the oloak-and-
bonnet show. The only ray of full-dress was
supplied by the ’Varsity Pooh-Bah and his fidus
Achates, who bared their linen to the breeze, and
gave the front row the air of waiting for the ballet
to come on. There was a top-heavy house, and
when the lion was let out by the Moderator (a
child of light, whose proud and ceaseless boast it
is that he was once kissed by Queen Victoria—at
a harmless age, however), the cheers wouldn’t have
disgraced a Melbourne Cup winner.
The much-advertised doctor’s debut was a suc-
cess, though his style of talking reminded me of a
person chipping shingles; and I didn’t find my
manners improved by his deportment, either, for
he points with his thumb over his shoulder and
moistens his finger on his lips in a way that would
suggest Bernhardt lip-salve in a less pious lecturer.
It was the largest smile I have ever seen outside a
minstrel show; for nature has endowed Dr. T.
with a mouth that looks lonely without a straight
bar in it; but there is no mistaking its geniality.
The only fault I found with the Talmage smile
(which will now find its way into every pulpit in
the country, I expect) was its irrelevancy. For
instance, it broke forth when Dr. T. was alluding
to woe and sudden death, with the incongruous
effect of a man winking violently at a corpse.
Dr. Talmage has a profile that reminds you of
every Pope’s head you have ever seen flattened
out on a Roman penny, and he wears his hair d la
French hen at the back, while a long wet look is
slicked over the top making a little island of his
occipital baldness. He doesn’t wear a choker, but
a turned-down collar and black bow, and it is only
when he rolls his eyes up to the gas-fittings that
you remember the kurruk.
I wonder why the management sooled the Pres-
byterian Moderator on to the platform to intro-
duce Talmage ? Fancy banding an audience over
to the uncertain mercies of a man who said that
“he could assure Dr. Talmage of his welcome ’’ in
the most top-lofty manner, and finished up with
the hope that they would be able “to see how he
does it ” —as if the magnetic doctor were ft sort of
clerical thimble-rigger who “ did it” with an im-
perceptible turn of the wrist, gentlemen I All
talky introducers are a mistake, and I lirmly
believe they have queered more promising shows
at the outset than they ever set on their legs. But
if you must have your star led on by the nose, as
it were, there ought to be a short, fixed formula
for the sponsor to gabble through with a death-
penalty attached to any original observations.
I am told that Dr. Talmage struck Sydney at a
very hungry time among the unco guid, and that
flocks of meenisters hung about his arrival like
crows round a poultry-yard, hoping to yard up the
good man for their own particular tabernacle.
Indeed, one of them said gleefully to the Much-
Travelled that Talmage had arrived in the nick
of time to boom a drooping kirk, and looked
wildly astonished when it was gently hinted to
him that it was not Dr. T.’s intention to go from
pulpit to pulpit preaching while Brother Hamish
M’Dougall pocketed the “ gate.”
“The Electric Club of N.S.W.” budded out
into a conversazione at Need’s Rooms, the other
Friday. I really must say I have never seen so
bored a lot of people as the women who wandered
among the groves thereof. I thought the working-
model of a Jules Yerne, cigar-shaped, under-sea
boat, invented by a Sydney man, was the most
solidly interesting thing on view—but, from a
butterfly standpoint, an organ playing “Faust,”
in a puffy sort of way, by electricity, and a man
cooking comestibles by the same “ disturbance of
molecular equilibrium,” as the scientists say, were
the airiest points about a very chippy outing.
I am sorry to see, too, that a lightning-grilled
chop can look just as coddled and sunburnt round
the edges as the less scientifically prepared victual,
and, with the keen consciousness of a dark and
hopeless culinary future, I regretfully noticed that
an electric muffin looks just as predisposed to turn
and rend you as any rule-of-thumb flap-jack in the
laud. Bub I think electric conversaziones ought
to be for men only. Tho’ there were music and
lightning-sketches and tea, still the women flagged
all round, and I don’t think a lady in pale grey
silk especially will attend another, for under the
remorseless glare of her electric-points her other-
wise undelectable make-up made her look as if her
cheeks had been dipped in mulberry-juice.
An epidemic of dances is upon us this month,
the most important being the always-popular St.
Vincent’s ball, on 7th inst, in Sydney Town Hall,
which promises to be a very gay affair under
creamy patronage. Mrs. J. T. Toohey and Miss
Mary Jennings are hon. secs., and the tickets are
going off with a pop. On Friday, 10th, the
annual ball of the East Sydney Rowing Club
takes place at West’s rooms and is almost sure to
be as hilarious as its predecessors. To-night
(Thursday) a Daffodil dance in aid of St. Francis’
Church takes the floor at the Paddington Town
Hall in the presence of Mayoress Manning, and on
30th Aug. a “violet” dance in aid of the Mater-
nity Home (Mrs. McManus and Miss M'Girr
hon. secs.) will probably attract a light-heeled
crowd.
One recent Sydney evening excitement was a
grand Military night at the Royal in aid of a 2nd
infantry reading and recreation-room, and held
under the aegis of Major Hutton, who was more of
an extinguisher on the proceedings than a crown
of glory. A clever programme had been drawn up
by the committee, bub the M.G. dashed his goose-
quill through item after item lest they should
detract from the success of the forthcoming
“ millinery ” manoeuvres, and the result was a
peptonised sort of entertainment eked out by
amateurs. It didn’t take four horses to carry
home the “gate.”
The newest thing unearthed in “ A Woman’s
Revenge,” Bland Holt’s final outbreak of realism
at Sydney Lyceum, was when Henrietta Watson
(whose incurable habit of letting herself loose
without a collar will give her pneumonia if she
tries it on at ’Ome) told Cosgrove that she
“ could only be a cousin to him.” Miss Watson,
whose stage wardrobe must be mounting sky-
high by this time, wore one good-looking yellow
dress nearly covered with cream lace and with big
yellow sleeves—just the right coloured gown to
play a jealous scene out in. But there isn’t much
scope for clothing in the drama, and really the
most desperately fashionable items were the black
velvet straps down the evening continuations of
Mr. Bland Holt, who seems to have struck the
secret of perennial youth, and is perkier every
time you see him.
Mrs. Bland Holt, as Lottie Bromley, was
brightly dressed in gladsome tints that suited the
complexion of the cheerful scenery in every
case. A gladiator-red gown with black satin
puffs was Mrs. Holt’s most successful toilet, but,
as I said before, “A Woman’s Revenge” is not
the sort of play to make a wardrobe-mistress turn
pale—the excitement rests with the scene-shifters.
At Sydney Lyceum on Saturday evening nearly
every lorgnette in the theatre was steadily aimed
’tween acts at the two artists’ models whom
J.C.W. has imported, regardless of expense, to
pose in the forthcoming tableaux vivants, which
will be sandwiched in during the opera season, for
the tableaux are not a whole show in themselves,
but require backing up on the bill. One of
the most effective pictures of the lot will
probably be the “Blind Girl of Pompeii,” in which
Miss Mona Rathbone—a Dianaesque sort of
damsel with faultless features, brown hair and
eyes, and a creamy skin like satin repp notepaper
(by gaslight at least)—has her best chance. Miss
Marie Rathbone, the other poseuse, has more pro-
nounced features, darker hair, and the shortest
upper lip I have ever seen. The physique of these
maidens is a nice change in a land of anaemia and
flat feet. Provided neither of the models sneezes
at a critical moment on the opening night, I take
it upon myself to prophesy that the tableaux will
be a boom from the beginning.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Molbourne Confidences.
Mklbouknk, July 31.
. Ever since the theatrical fete, the other Wed-
nesday, I have been haunted by recollections of a
heliotrope - complexioned woman who bloomed
upon a front bench of the members’ stand. The
artist who got the contract for facial repairs and
decorations must have mistaken lavender for pink
in mixing his colours. Or possibly the lady’s
natural epidermis, blue with cold, showing through
a too-transparent enamel coating, was responsible
for the phenomenon. In a fernery on a hot-wind
day she might have captured a very young man’s
imagination by her weird pallor, but it was not a
successful winter effect. One couldn’t help think-
ing of the “cold, strange eyes of a little mer-
maiden and the gleam of her golden hair," and
similar uncanny phases of female loveliness. It
was quite a relief to escape into the refreshment-
tents, for there the attendant nymphs were as rosy
as Nature intended them to be—in some cases,
perhaps, a little more so.
Nellie Stewart was, of course, the crowning
attraction, and very fetching she looked in dark-
green corded velvet shot with red in such a
manner as to throw over the surface of the
material a ruddy sheen like the reflection of fire-
light. The gown fitted Miss Ellen’s trim figure
like a glove, and a sweet French toque, of grass-
green velvet and Czar violets, nestled daintily
above a most successful version of the Princess
May fringe. In place of the wig-like point coming
down in the middle of the forehead, the same
effect was obtained by a soft, little centre-curl,
looking delightfully innocent and unpremeditated.
Florence Young was handsome and passive,
as usual, in blue serge, pink blouse and sailor
hat, and Ida Osborne looked better than
she has ever looked off the boards before,
in a black gown and dear little bonnet with
touches of green. Meanwhile, “Mr.” I. Osborne
was out in the open, bearing their infant daughter
aloft with much paternal pride.
The proprietresses of this spacious tent didnt
attempt to play down to the masses, but stood
entrenched behind their counter, giving all their
attention to the exigencies of a pushing trade.
The chieftainess showed that her eyes could flash
when an attendant mislaid a relay of spoons.
This anxiety to dispense her wares decently and
in order was a pleasing contrast to the free-and-
easy methods of some of the other tents, where
customers helped themselves to sugar with their
fingers and were smilingly requested to stir their
high-priced brews with pipe-stems or hat-pins as
the case might be.
Next door, May Pollard, cosy in soft, grey furs
and an artistic feathered hat, was in tune with
herself and all the world. Little Vi. Varley,
handicapped by the fact of a hubby and a rather
unbecoming brown frock, with petunia velvet
sleeves, apparently found the cigarette business
no sinecure.
In an adjoining booth, Maud Williamson, pale
and picturesque in unrelieved black, entertained
an admiring court, somewhat to the detriment of
the tea-traffic. A sister saleswoman, with pink
roses in her hat, stepped into the breach and
humanely fed the less interesting visitors, while
active Dora Mostyn, assisted by a very pretty
girl in green velvet (whom I discovered to be
Lilia Wilde, when I was told), worked with a will
behind the counter. Every shilling that poured
into the tills was profit, it seems. Light refresh-
ments, cigarettes and cigars had been contributed
to the Fund by Melb. firms, in open defiance of
ordinary business principles.
The first man to annex the degree of Litt. D.
(Doctor of Letters), lately created by Melb.
University, is now bent on studying Law. Un-
fortunately, he has access to no pocket but his
own poorly-lined one, and is, moreover, a newly-
wedded benedict. However, his bride, whose tastes
are equally intellectual, sympathises heart and soul
with his schemes for raising the wherewithal, and
has undertaken to preside over the book-shop they
have opened in a Melb. suburb, until hubby shall
have realised his ambition. Books required for
the various University courses, form a specialty
of the stock which, as a whole, has been chosen to
suit the needs of fellow-students, who are rolling
up in such force that J uliet is nearly run off her
feet.
The “Helping Hands” have apparently taken a
new lease of life. A few nights ago, in the home
suburb of one of the members, the company of
genteel strollers received quite an ovation. This
long-delayed recognition is said to have thawed
for the nonce the hauteur of the lavender-gloved,
crush-hatted manager, who, as a rule, finds his
only solace in inditing ironical letters to the Press.
The second part of the H.H. performance con-
sists of a rough-and-tumble rendering of “Trial
by Jury. ” G ilbert’s operatta inclines to burlesque
at any time, but if the chorus enunciated the
words so as to give point to their overpowering by-
play, and the soloists could make themselves
heard above the prevailing din, the H.H.
version wouldn’t be such arrant tomfoolery
as it is at present. The damsel cast for
the plaintiff had hitherto made unobtrusive
appearances at occasional amateur concerts. Em-
boldened by operatic experience, however, she has
taken to rendering her little ditties with vast play
of expression and variety of action. .The sayings
of Molloy’s “Waggoner” to his sweetheart, for
example, are emphasised by becoming little moves,
fancy eye-twinklings and coquettish tosses of the
head. All very pretty and “naughty,” no doubt,
but quite foreign to one’s conception of “ Carrier
John.”
1 have not come across anyone who has seen
Lady Hopetoun’s nose since the accident, but we
are loyally hoping for the best. The Age made a
rash announcement that the noble patient would
bob up serenely at some races, immediately after
leaving Dr. Fitzgerald’s private hospital. This
must have been the hypothesis of a man, probably
a reckless bachelor, who judged the case by the
recuperative powers of his own proboscis when it
has got damaged in action. The Countess, one
supposes, will consider herself “not presentable”
for some time to come, even though the bones be
just as they were, and the stitches have left very
little pinkness behind. Besides, she naturally
“funks” going abroad to be the object of a
“popular demonstration.” Lady Loch, the wily,
would have revelled in the opportunity, but this
diffident daughter of Erin, if the truth were
known, watches the recovery of her leading
feature with terror in her round, dark eye.
VAN M.
A great effort is being made to disfranchise
3000 Melb. rate payers under the Purification of
Rolls*Act. An unadulterated “ roll ” would con-
sist entirely of upper crust.
Wolseley has been made a British field-
marshal at the unusually early age of 61. Very
few British field-marshals attain their exalted rank
until they are 80 or more, and the post is generally
regarded as a reward for living till one doesn’t
know enough to die off out of the road.
Conan Doyle is wanted in Melbourne to
golve the mystery of the man at Scott’s hotel, who
is supposed to have suicided by stabbing him-
self thirteen times round the heart with a dagger,
which he subsequently replaced in its case. Then
he died. It was time.
Parkes to the last clung to the hope that
Governor Duff would pass over Reid’s undoubted
right to leadanewN.S.W. Ministry in favour of his
Hair. When that hope finally left him, he made
a great struggle to drape his toga decently. “Hi
ham very pleased hat being relieved from the
responsibility. Hi will never take hoffice again.”
Nasty, nasty grapes !
The sudden resignation of the Dibbs
Ministry was ostensibly brought about by the flat
refusal of Governor Duff to appoint seven new
Legislative Councillors. There are about a dozen
vacancies in the Council, and Dibbs claimed
the right to fill seven of these vacancies.
Governor Duff, however, refused, on no constitu-
tional grounds that can be discovered. According
to all precedent his conduct was unaccountable,
even English custom being entirely against him.
The first wedding has taken place at Cool-
gardie, and the local mining press rises to the
occasion. “ The bride wore a rich, creamy, quartz-
coloured silk. Miss Hickey was dressed in a
reddish substance, with ironstone-coloured leaders
running round the main body. Mrs. Feehan
wore a pale milky quartz combination, with gold
outcrops the full breadth of the face. Miss
Brennan waltzed round in a slate-coloured robe,
the principal outcrops being decorated with diorite-
coloured stringers, and other surface indications
of a highly-pleasing nature.”
Dear Bulletin, —Much of your Boldre-
wood criticism is doubtless correct, but he scarcely
deserves all your disparagement; for, although his
style may be cumbrous and his plots puerile, he
certainly scores with his descriptions of Australian
scenery, manners, and customs. The ruck of
Australian stories convey ridiculous ideas of this
country, as you know. Boldrewood is, at all
events, true to life. He could never pourtray a
character like North’s in “ His Natural Life,” but,
for all that, there are times when he carries an
Australian with him. Take, for example, the
night ride from Berrima gaol in “ Robbery Under
Arms,” or the death of the insurrectionist leader
in “ The Miner’s Right.” Many of his heroes are
commonplace, and his style is sometimes exe-
crable, but the truth of his descriptions more than
compensates for these defects,—Yours, H.A.
Vic. draper - diplomatist Reid probably
knows by this time that about 250 per cent, of
him is draper, and only the remainder diplo-
matist. The British authorities, it would seem,
accuse him of having blabbed to the smart
Yankees who are running the Hawaiian Govt.,
with the result that President Dole and Co. sent
out a steamer and annexed Necker Island, so
badly wanted as a depot by the new Imperial cable
company. Fresh from Downing-street, bursting
with vanity, Reid had to talk or die. The Hono-
lulu Yankees listened, and secured the prize,
which is now for sale at a fancy prize. Reid’s
knighthood is gone for ever.
Mr. Samuel Albert Rosa, the loud-
mouthed person who wrecked theN.S.W. Labour-
party at the Bar won, writes denying the state-
ment that had he been elected he could not have
taken his seat, and adding that he was not run by
any clique, but was “the duly selected candidate
of the Bar won branch of the L.E.L., the members
of the branch relying upon Macdonnell’s frequent
written promise that he would not oppose but
would assist their candidate.’’ The idea of Mac-
donnell, who scored over 500 and came within
two votes of winning the seat, having promised
not to oppose but to assist the miserable outsider
who could only scrape up 43 suffrages all told, is a
beautiful specimen of cheek. Also, the idea of
this latter obscure straggler claiming to be the
“duly selected candidate” of the party which
■voted so unanimously for the other man is still
more excellent. Mr. Rosa’s services to the N.S.W.
Democratic-party are that, with no reasonable
chance of winning himself, he split the Labour-
vote just enough to prevent the real Labour-can-
didate getting in, and The Bulletin trusts that
the fact will be duly remembered wherever and
whenever this blatant person again offers his
help to the country. . . ...


The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co., Limited, reports :
Wool.— ln the local market a satisfactory business has
been done during the past week at firm prices. Supplies
have consisted principally of scoured skin wools, which
realised from 10Jd. to lljd. per lb. for top sorts of good
average quality and condition, and up to 14|d. per lb.
for very superior clean-washed samples. The London
sales (fourth series) closed on 25th ult. without any further
change in values. Altogether, 216,000 bales were sold
during the series, and 78,000 bales have been held over
for future disposal. The fifth series of sales will com-
mence in the home market on 18th September.

witness to the signature is the wife of the Rev.
Mcßride, Wesleyan Minister, of Kyneton.

Personal Items.
“Queen Victoria is exceedingly fond of
birds.” — English. Oof birds?
Rudyard Kipling has in his veins, it is
stated, 25 per cent, dark blood.
Of the barristers on the roll of N.S. Wales
in 1863 only two —Salomons and Simpson—are
in active practice.
“I have quite settled down,” writes Agent-
General Playford from London. Then there must
have been a landslip, for Tom weighs three tons
nett.
Another old Hawkesbury native, Wm.
Hall, born in the reign of Gov. Macquarie, died
at Windsor, N.S.W., last week. He never once
in all his long life left the locality where he was
born.
A curious thing about the denunciation of
spiritualistic humbug in Argus leaders is the way
they have covertly sneered at James Smith, a
veteran member of the Argus staff, who is also a
spookite.
Douglas Sladen is now on the staff of
Jerome K. Jerome’s piratical paper, To-Day,
where, behind his pen-name of “ Bookseller,” he
slates The Bulletin, from which his rag so un-
blushingly steals.
Mr. Tom Roberts has just finished a
strong and characteristic portrait of the inevitable
Mr. H. Parkes. As a statesman Mr. Parkes has
been a shocking frost; but he may live in history
as a painter’s model.
Philip Gavan Duffy, one of Charles Gavan
Duffy’s sons, was among the recently retrenched
Vic. Government servants, and last week he left
the province to accept office under a more kindly
power—the King of Siam.
Good-young-man Kirton, M.L.A., of
Ballarat, thinks that the Woman’s Franchise
League would be unwise to appoint a prominent
temperance person as president. These children
of light are getting wily in their regeneration.
The accident to Lady Hopetoun’s pretty
little nose is not without its advantages. Since
the “injured organ” has been bound up she has
been relieved from the eternal insistance of the
most predominant characteristic of Smellbourne.
A strong feeling exists among Adelaide
clergymen that the successor to Bishop Kennion
should he selected from Australian churchmen.
But, unfortunately, in the diocese of Adelaide
quite a shoal of parsons each thinks he ought to
be chosen.
M.L. Governoress Glasgow has been dis-
tinguishing herself at Niagara by shooting in a
raft one of the tunnels which utilises the power of
the Falls. The mile was done in 80 seconds, and
the lady said they were the liveliest 80 seconds she
had ever experienced. This may please Gov.
Glasgow—or it may not.

The reigning beauty
in Melb. now is Mrs.
Morris (a daughter of Dr.
Wilmott, of Butchers’-
row), who is really, with-
out exaggeration, a. won-
derfully lovely woman —
with the kind of beauty
about which poets rhapso-
dise and painters rave.
She is said to be in-
tellectual, too. She has
a slightly-aquiline nose,
a prominent chin, fine
eyes, and an apple-blos-
som skin. Her husband
is partner in a firm of
wholesale ironmongers.
The Bulletin hears
from London that “ A
Yellow Aster,” in the
first week of its 7s. 6d.
edition, sold—7ooo copies.
The authoress, in that
week, netted .£825, and
yet she says the only real
pleasure she has derived
from the book was the
chance to “ cut dead ” a
Melbourne moneyed snob
who was once rude to her.
Young Kir ton, M.P. for Ballarat (Vic.),
was one night strutting about the Parliamentary
lobbies in a new suit, presented to him by the pro-
prietors of the Ballarat cloth mills. He was
desirous of doing the firm a good turn and so
solicited the opinion of everybody as to the suit.
At last he met a well-known pressman, and can-
vassed him for a compliment. “What do you
think of this for Ballarat make?” he said. “Well,
Kirton, to tell you the truth,” replied the genial
journalist, “ I think it a slop-made suit on a slop-
made man.”
Up to date not one of the Agents-General
who have represented S. A. in London have lived
to return to Adelaide.
Living S.A. knights are: Ayers, Coles,
Colton, Davenport, Downer, Elder, Milne, Smith
and Todd, with Baker, two Boothbys and Goyder
as O.M.G.’s. Those who have carried their
baubles to the tetter land are: Blyth, Bray,
Fisher, Hughes, Hanson, Jeffcot, Kingston,
Morphett, Morgan, Rosa and Torrens, with Hart
in the alleged lower grade.
Forster is one of Melbourne’s noblemen.
Twelve years ago, he started his Try Excelsior
Society, described as being in Toorak, but really
over the border in democratic Prahran, and
devoted to the welfare of poor boys. It is really
marvellous what he has accomplished. He never
seeks newspaper commendation, and very seldom
gets it. Now he has 500 boys.
G. P. Smith, the man whom Supple tried
to shoot, was once talking in Vic. Assembly when
gigantic Patrick Hanna, member for Moama,
became offensive in interrogation. Turning to
the dignified Charles Macmahon, the orator
said : “Mr. Speaker, I would esteem it a favour
if you would silence the grand P. Hanna of the
Assembly.” Then there was a laugh which
effectively shut up the Irishman.
Law, Labour M.L.A, for Balmain South —
pledge brand—is selling off his drapery business,
and lately said to a Sydney pressman : “Try a
cheap hat, you’ve a big head and this is the last
I’ve left —couldn’t find a customer, for the people
about here have very small heads.” “ That
accounts for the fools they made of themselves
last Tuesday,” said the scribe, then unaware that
he was addressing the people’s elected.
The story goes, says “ Will Honeycomb,”
that, on the way over from America, a young
fellow-passenger approached Pastor Talmage and
told him that many people on board would take it
as a favour if he would give them a lecture. “ I
guess,” said the Doctor, carefully surveying his
interviewer from head to foot, “I guess, young
man, you’ve come to fish in the wrong pond.”
“ I think not,” said the latter, “ the mistake is in
my having failed to use the right bait.”
M‘iNeill, the travelling preacher, is taking
the wind out of Talmage’s sails in Melbourne. He
has a “singing evangelist,” one Burke, with him.
On their first appearance they secured the Con-
gregational church, and so many people rolled up
that they had to put some into the Presbyterian
church opposite, and conduct a service there.
Burke officiated at both churches, dodging back-
wards and forwards and singing furiously. The
Town Hall was then engaged, and as it is found
that that will not hold the people, the Exhibition
has been hired. If that won’t hold them —well,
perhaps the Lord will provide.
Parkes, presiding at a pump lecture en-
titled “O, sir, it was the drink that did it!”: —
I have entered my 80th year, and feel strong and
healthy, because I have lived an abstemious life. I lived
to the age of 30 without knowing the taste of strong
drink, and I attribute my health to this abstemiousness
in early life. ... If you study Dr. Ward Richard-
son’s book you will see that human nature only wants one
beverage, and that is water.
This is the old gentleman who takes wine for
his stomach’s sake every day of his life.
This is the man of whom a co-banqueter
once told The Bulletin: “By Jove! I don’t

Dear Bulletin,— You have made a
mistake about the largest number of sheep to
be shorn in any one N.S.W. shed this season.
Wingadee heads the list with 266,000; East
Toorale is next with 250,000 ; Dunlop next,
230,000; and others with 200,000 and over.—
Geo. Russell, Lila Springs.
A Sydney shopkeeper couldn’t get a
tobacconist to pay up a “little account,” .£45.
So he wrote suggesting that the latter should send
him 2oz. of tobacco daily until the contra squared
matters. Later, the twain met. “I got your
note.” “Yes? But I didn’t get my tobacco.”

“The heaviest bullock in Victoria, and
perhaps in Australia, is owned by a Warrnambool
butcher. It is about five years old, and weighs
27861 b. (1 ton 4cwt. 7st. —only Ist. less than 1J

Once more the field of magazine writing is
to be tried in Australia. “ Cosmos” is the title
decided upon by a publishing company that has
been floated to run the monthly. Probably
Ernest Blackwell, with his “ Centennial” ex-
perience, could choke off Scott, the editor, from
attempting to run a similar venture. Jerome (not
Jerome K. Jerome) is the business manager of the
new periodical. The whole of the September
number is contributed gratis.
Travelling from Brisbane to Maryborough
(Q.) by train some time ago, at every station the
wild and hairy Labour-member, Hoolan, was seen
parading with a squalling infant. By-and-bye,
the Mary River bad to be crossed by boat, the
bridge being down, and Hoolan with the baby
and a load of parcels was followed by a woman
with a couple more howling children. On reach-
ing Maryborough, the woman, a local resident,
asked Tooth, M.P., the name of her unknown
chevalier. “Hoolan,” he explained. “And,”
remarks Nicholas Tooth, “that woman’s husband
has to vote the Labour-ticket now.”
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
The bulk of wool offered this week consisted of scoured.
Only a few lots of greasy came under the hammer, this
being the moat “ off ” month of the off season.
From a cable just received from our London agents we
gather that the Rite rise of about one-halfpenny per
pound is sustained for medium and first-class light-condi-
tioned greasy wool. Heavy and inferior being a little
easier.
Latest news from America is not quite so encouraging,
but it is still probable the free admission of wool to the
United States will, in the near future, be an accomplished
fact.
Shearing is progressing without impediment, and
already buyers are beginning to scent the new wool far
off, and are eager to pay good prices for any early lots
they can pounce upon.
We hope growers who hitherto have shipped to London
will try the Sydney market in the coming season. So
much has been written on this point that owners must see
the wisdom of selling here. Those who wish to read up
the subject we refer to our “Wool Growers’ Annual,”
which we will forward anywhere free, and to the able
articles recently appearing in the Wool and Produce
Journal.
Sydney, 27th July, 1894.
From Jan. 1 to May 5, ’94, there were 50
lynchings in the U.S. —the victims being always
negroes. The general Southern excuse is that
the whites are roused to madness by offences
against women, but in 41 Southern cases only 9
alleged crimes came in this class, the others being
mostly trivial offences. Procedure varies. Some-
times the lynchers soak a man with kerosene, stick
him full of splinters, and set fire to him ; some-
times they put a woman in a cask stuck with nails
and roll her downhill. The perpetrators of these
outrages are never brought to justice. “’Tis the
star-spangled banner, O long may it wave o’er the
land of the free and the home of the brave.”
Dion Boucicault’s
brother, Arthur Leslie
Bouroicault (he insisted on
spelling it with an “ r”)
died in Sydney last week—
-72. He was on Melb.
Argus in the forties;
founded Rockhampton
Argus when the Port
Curtis rush broke out; sold
out, and bought a brewery;
sold out, and bought Gym-
pie Miner ; sold out, and
bought Shepparton (Vic.)
Argus ; burnt out, and
bought a little Burwood
(Syd.) paper, which he was
running when he died. In
his youth he had a pretty
wit; and even later he
crushed one loathsome con-
temporary by calling the
editor —a sour, lank man
—“a walking emetic.”
He knew to a penny what
a newspaper should cost to
produce, and invariably
saved the penny. Travel-
ling showmen revered his
name, and always gave
him their dodgers.
A Frenchman has
been imported to London
to help Burney Young in
the management of S.A.
Wine and Produce Depot,
and it is suggested that his
spare hours shall be de-
voted to teaching dancing
and deportment to Agent-
General Playford, so that
that official “may do
credit to his country at
Court functions.” It is
’further affirmed that, apro-
pos of recent verbal indis-
cretions, Premier Kingston
wrote a hurried note in
these words to his ancient
crony—“ Dear Tom, —For
God’s sake hold your blanky jaw ! ”
Dear Bulletin, —There is just now a
great outcry against the Sydney publican who
“puts a bead on” his inferior liquor by adul-
terating it with sulphuric acid. Yet all the
sulphuric acid necessary to perform that service
for a bottle of snakejnice would be perfectly harm-
less. Sulphuric acid is an A 1 refrigerant, stimu-
lant, digestive, and several other nice things. The
real trouble is not with the small modicum of acid
sold. It is with the vile, unrectified white spirit,
full of horrible impurities and methylated jim-
jams. “ Them’s my sediments.” —Yours, H.C.
To Our Wives and Mothers.
The health of woman is the foundation of beauty.
Good health insures beautiful symmetrical lines
and forms, the healthy and blooming appearance,
which even brings charm to irregular features.
Beauty without health is impossible, conse-
quently it is obvious that women should pay
greatest attention to retain good health. Un-
fortunately, women are greatly exposed to dis-
orders which cause untold suffering and prevent
them from fulfilling their home, as well as social,
duties, making life a burden. >
The greatest medical authorities teach that
female troubles would not occur if the kidneys
and liver were kept in a healthy condition. It
would be useless to attempt to cure female diseases
without removing the actual cause. Warner’s
Safe Cure, in establishing a healthy action of
the kidneys and liver, and neutralising the
poisonous acids in the blood —the cause of all in-
flammation, removes the germ of ulceration,
abscesses, &c. This remedy is, therefore, invalu-
able in the successful treatment of all female com-
plaints, and its virtue is proclaimed by Mrs.
Eliza Parkes, of Gisborne, victoria, under date
May 26th, 1894, who states : —“About six years
ago I was taken very ill, and was confined to my
bed for several months, being attended by the
doctor who resided here at that time, whom I
believe to be a very clever and skilful physician,
and most certainly a very careful and attentive
one. After doing all he could for me, he gave me
no hope whatever of my recovering, and stated
that I was suffering from diseased kidneys, the
liver also was in a very bad state, the action of
the heart very weak and the blood very poor, and
which he termed similar to molasses. My legs
were swollen to a very great size. Having heard
from friends about your Safe Cure, I determined
to try it. I bought one bottle at a time, and found
it was doing me good, so I continued taking it,
and after having taken several bottles was able to
get out of bed. Still continuing the medicine I
soon became strong enough to get about as well as
ever I was. This I consider a wonderful cure, as
my age is now 76 years, and I have not had the
least symptoms of the old complaint since I left
off taking the medicine. I informed the doctor
that I was using Warner’s Safe Cure, and he
recommended me to continue, as he said it was
a very good medicine.”
Witness, L. McBRIDE.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“ Panderer Reid.”—Dibbs.
“ Somersaulter Dibbs.”— Reid.
Birth and berth—the same word.

Guelph.
The two most distinguished lobbyists of
the late N.S.W. Assembly—men run after by
every syndicate—failed to secure re-election,
Caldwell, M.P., has a Bill before the S.A-
Assembly providing for emasculation in the case of
men found guilty of certain classes of assaults.
“We Freetraders are the majority in this
province,” says a S.M. Herald correspondent.
That is not true, for the “ Freetraders” aren’t game
to institute Freetrade.
Jim Toohey, at Tam worth (N.S.W.), had
a committee of 38 in the town itself. He polled
there 12 votes. Twenty-six Judas Iscariots !
Human nature is a great institution.
Q, Parliamentary refreshment-room has
substituted waitresses for waiters. Receipts will
probably gain, and members’ morals cannot very
well lose. But where is the W.C.T.U. ?
“I have held the highest position in the
land and have obtained the highest honors given
to an Australian by the British Govt.” Who
said this? Parkes? No, Dibbs—at Tamworth.
They all talk so-fashion after visiting London.
Forty M.L. shearers have gone to Queens-
land to do the work the strikers refuse. Many
more are going. It is 1891 over again—fury,
fizzle, flight. Champion has just returned to
Australia in time to see another procession of
“lions led by asses.”
Powers, M.P., taunts Q. Government with
the fact that their policy does not disclose the
encouragement of any other industry besides
sugar and sheep, “ and I don’t think that sugar
and sheep have ever been better represented than
by the present Ministry.”
Nothing more is heard of that idea (deli-
cately mooted in a London paper) to convert the
Imperial Institute into a headquarters of
Freemasonry. It was an excellent idea, for
Freemasonry is the most Himperial institution
that ever kept itself dark.
Cunnamulla (Q.) is to have a railway, not
because it is Cunnamulla, but because the railway
will be another feeder to Brisbane monopoly. Were
Cunnamulla anywhere north of Bundaberg the
Southern Ministry would shriek “ Empty
Treasury ! ” and Hng-bark all the trees through
30 deg. of latitude.

The forfcy-eighthday
of the second Speight v.
Symecasefound Wheeler,
M.L. A., ex-Minister of

Speight was dismissed
at the command of the
Age. Guileless Wheeler
really can’t say whether
the almighty penny rag
had influenced political
matters, and he
(Wheeler !) attaches the
same importance to the
Age as to the Licensed
Victuallers’ Gazette.
Half-an-hour was wasted
in a cross-examination
of Wheeler’s profound
ignorance, yet Speight’s
counsel retrained from at-
tempting a home thrust.
He might have reminded
Wheeler that Speight
was “suspended” in a
violent hurry directly
after he had issued a
writ against Syme, and
he might have read one
of Deakin’s parliamen-
tary speeches, describing
the Shiels regime as
“ Government by a news-
paper.” They fight this
dreary battle with big
buttons on the end of the
foils.
They were representatives, of two sugar-
districts, one being actually a land-valuer under
the ISugar Guarantee Act, who moved ana
seconded the Address-in-Reply to the Governor s
Speech in Bananaland Assembly. There is more
in this than meets a flitting eye.
The Aiiaps
The Suffrage!is not a right, but it is a privilege.
Likewise, breathing is ,not a right, it is a privi-
lege ; and sunlight is not a right, it is a privilege.
Also, listening to Tory twaddle is not an inflic-
tion, it is a duty and a pleasure.
Nesbit, Q.C., the Adelaide lawyer, is still
in London, .and it is suggested that, acting on a
Ministerial tip, lie is waiting for the Privy Coun-
cil appeal in re the boundary dispute with Vic-
toria, which S. A. Assembly has decided shall be
settled by legal means if negotiations fail.
Among other.S. A. economies, the penny-
a-day good-service allowance has been taken from
the police, who also lose, as before stated, their
periodical Sunday leave. What makes the par-
tiality of the penalty more apparent is that by the
influence of the Labour members all other workers
at less than £l5O a year—both on the pro. tem.
and the fixed lists—in the railway workshops
and other Government employment are totally
exempt from any retrenchment under the Public
Salaries Bill.
No inconsiderable factor in the defeat of
Millionaire Burdekin for the Hawkesbury was
the vote of the newly-established Pitt Town
Labour Settlement. Mr. Burdekin, now out m
the cold, has not and never had any-
thing to recommend him but his geni-
ality and his money. His successful
opponent, attorney Morgan, will never
be a Talleyrand or a Disraeli, but he
sings nicely and that’s something.
Burdekin for years acted as fairy
godmother to the “Great N.S.W. Freetrade
Party,” which he boarded and lodged and found
in drinks. Also, he paid for the ironing of the
Great N.S.W. Freetrade Party’s hat and the
patching of its clothes, and for levelling its
boot-heels. What has happened to Burdekin
by reason of the settlement in his electorate of a
labour-colony, is shortly going to happen to Mr.
Staughton, the holder of a Victorian pocket-
borough.
Kingsbury, M.L.A. (Q.): k ‘A man of 31
has 10 years more experience than a man of 21,
and I think he should, therefore, have a second
vote.” Also, a man of 51 has 30 years more ex-
perience than a man of 21, and should, conse-
quently (on this basis), be entitled to four votes.
Logic is logic.
Mahomet and the Mountain once more.
The Argus actually sought out the once-despised
Trenwith for his opinion of the N.S.W. elections.
The would-be Radical-cum-Conservative journal
almost recognises Trenwith as the real Opposition
leader now, and reports him far more freely than
has been its wont.
The S.A. Assembly is divided into parties
thus : The Speaker, the Chairman of Committees,
11 Ministerialists, including five members of the
Govt., the Whip, 21 legislators belonging to the
“ Independent Country Party,’’ 10 Labour-m 'in-
ters, seven Oppositionists, two Independent mem-
bers and Bartlett.
In view of the Parkes blight threatening,
it is well to remember some of the men who helped
to constitute the Parkes Govt, in 1887 : Mil burn
Creek Garrett, A. B. Co. Abigail, Hornsby Burns,
Anglo-Australian Clarke, and so on. Ye gods !
And what Parkes has done once he would do
again if he dared and could.
Jawbone Neild’s barrackers must be funny
dogs. They sprang a “ surprise ’’-party on him
the other night by singing “ Wait till the turn of
the tide!” As if poor Jawbone, rejected at w.b.
Wales general election, hadn’t got “surprise
enough when he found that the tide had turned
—dead against him at the poll.
Michael Duffy, M.P. for Bundaberg and
ex-ferry boatman, has made himself briefly famous
in Q. Assembly by enunciating this opinion
“With so many heirs to the throne, I hardly
know why the Duke of York’s son should call tor
special recognition in the Speech ; but, as a loyal
subject, I congratulate their Royal Highnesses on
the birth of a son.”

barristerial incomes to take less than halt the
money on the Bench. Now, did any man over
take a judgeship for any other reason than a
calculation of his pecuniary advantage? lue
ablest barrister earning the Australian maxumrn,
not more than .£BOOO nowadays, _ may find Ins
career dashed to pieces irreparably in one instant,

Max Hirsch says that the cause of the
world’s depression is bimetallism, ex-Prenmr
Shiela that it is monometallism, ordinary people
that it is no-metallism.
Forty-five National Ass candidates were
returned at last N.S. W. election, and 52 were
rejected. The Ass, however, adopted candidates
without their permission, and many of them,
when they heard that they had been cursed by its
support, repudiated it and all its works, so it is
difficult to decide whether the animal has been
successful or not.
A correspondent: — Though the N.S.W.
Freetraders had in several electorates four, five and
even six candidates running for the one seat, they
did not lose one seat by the splitting of votes in
such electorates. The Protectionists, on the other
hand, lost eight seats through the multiplicity of
candidates—the elected men in these electorates
represent the minority.—F.T.
The declaration of Arthur Griffith, newly-
elected member for Waratah, “ that the N.S.W.
Labour-party would support Beelzebub himself if
he promised and could give the reforms de-
manded,” corresponds exactly with the historical
statement of George Black when first elected to
N.S.W. Assembly, that the Labour-party’s policy
Was “support in return for concessions.”
Talmage’s statement that Freetrade is
best suited to some countries and Protection to
others, according to their circumstances, has in-
furiated “ Outis,” of Sydney D.T., who calls Tal-
mage many different kinds of language. “ Outis’s”
theory evidently is that the same treatment applies
in every case, whether the patient has fallen over-
board from a ship or has been bitten by a horse.
It is characteristic of Vic. Premier Patter-
son that while he justifies the payment of huge
pensions for very scanty services to absentees like
Childers, Duffy, and Andrew Clarke, because of
the ill-effect which default might have on the
British pawnbroker, he contemplates repudiation
in the case of public officials who have worked
hard for 40 or 50 years, because they are under his
thumb. The butcher, the braggart, and the syco-
phant are manifest in many of his actions.

the Flood, always in stock. Constituencies misrepre-

Melbourne coroner Youl abases mothers
for dressing their children in flannelette instead of
woollen goods, the former being very liable to
take fire; whereupon the Argus makes a Free-
trade mother reply:—
Please, sir, the Government won’t let poor people’s
children wear flannel. The Government says that only
rich men’s children shall use wool. The Government
makes our poor little things wear cotton.
Yes, but give the Patterson his due ; if he has
forced inflammable dress material upon the poor,
backed by the “thrift” party, ho has deprived
them of fires, and so cleverly averted the danger.
Spagnoletti ; “ I have just been talking to
a Melb. civil servant in a responsible position, and
got a kind of per contra. He says: ‘ All our
privileges will go by the board, and we will be
reduced to the simple footing of master and ser-
vant. X see it sticking out. It is like the dis-
establishment of a State Church. Well, I’m not
sorry. I have been thwarted in the course of pro-
motion, and, trusting to my own ability, can see
clearly enough that I would have risen much
higher under an absolutely free system. The
tools to him that can use them and let us be like
Napoleon’s soldiers, each carrying the Marshal’s
baton in his knapsack. No man in Melbourne can
fill my office without special training, such as I
have undergone for years.’ ”
Dear Bulletin, —Re S.A. landsales. A
few weeks ago a block of GOOO acres of good land
well watered, improved, and within the line ot
coastal rains—was hawked about at 225. Gd. an
acre. The Govt, received far more money for it
in the fifties. Last week the Govt, had an offer of
65,000 acres of first-class agricultural land-fenced
in and improved close to a town and railway—the
price asked being the money paid them 40 years
ago by the owner’s uncle when the land was 50
miles from town and without a railway. ihose
who have been howling about the “unearned in-
crement” are dead against the purchase as it
would upset their pet schemes. The owner
doesn’t ask for cash, but will take Treasury 4 per
cent, bonds. Please let this go down per contra.—
Constant Reader, S. Australia.
In acknowledging an address from some
school girls the other day the ancient fox, Parkes,
pathetically spoke of “ the snows of 80 years
as if the snows of 80 years were not now and liad
not long been almost his whole stock-in-trade.
The other night, after the polling, he was carted
round Sydney by Joey Carruthers and Barney
Wise to show the “ snows ” aforesaid and to indi-
cate that at least one cartload of the Parkes
Ministry was quite ready. The snows caused
great enthusiasm and were loudly cheered as the
old man lifted his tile and showed the bald patch.
As The Bulletin has frequently said, clip and
shave Parkes and you’ll solve the political difficul-
ties of N.S.W. within five minutes. By the way,
H.P. in the letter to the aforesaid school-girls
speaks gloomily of his “ nearness to the grave.
Which would lead some people to believe that he
wasn’t quite so sure of shoo-ing off G. H. Reid
from the Foreign-trade leadership, as he gammoned
to be in public.
A pair of parsons dining at a fashion-
able Sydney hotel, the other evening, are
said to have come to blows over the distri-
bution of the Orange vote at Rand wick,
where Barton was thrown out. The Yellow
Pup barked ostensibly for Kinnane, but
really for Storey ; and the Baptist and Pres-
byterian dogmas were alike forgotten in the
heat of quarrel about the alleged treachery.
Dear Bulletin, —Dr. Bachelor, of
Dunedin (M.L.), ascribes drunkenness to
an organic defect in the brain, but pro
phesies that the disease is dying out as
humanity progresses in development. He
further theorises that, if prohibition shou d
stamp out drunkenness, it will cause the
general cultivation of other vices in the
stead of drunkenness. But, _as far as I
have scope to judge, experience prove-
that prohibition has never yet stamped
out drunkenness, but, rather, has multi-
plied it exceedingly. From various statis-
tics that have dazzled my admiration 1
feel inclined to lay down this natural Jin-
de-siecle law:—Drunkenness increases in
the ratio of the cube of the volume of
prohibition applied against it. —Yours,
The D.

O’Loghlen’s Judgeship has been a rod in
pickle, in Victoria, these ten years, and it is w*_l!
to settle, once for all, that the people never will
stand such an imposition. The Tubby Kerb-r<i
job savors yet. O’Loghlen is in no way qualified
for a judge. All the brethren of the bench will
rise up against him, and every barrister, led by
Purves. These political appointments are a curse.
Another thing, O’Loghlen would get sick on hard
work, sooner than Williams, Hood, or any oi
them. If Judge Holroyd retires, give him kud. s
for sticking to his work” like the traditional good
old Molesworth and Higinbotham. The idea is
to get the lumbering four-decker, O’Loghlen, in
for half a year during Hood’s holiday, then for
life, on Holroyd’s retirement.
A candidate for a N.S.W. country seat
deputed two local residents to act as stipendiary
scrutineers for him at two outside polling-places—
giving them, at the same time, most explicit in
structions to lodge their votes in his (the candi-
date’s) favour, ere taking their seats in the booth.
When the returns came in, the candidate found,
by reason of the figure 0 against his name at both
places, that neither scrutineer had voted for him—
each, no doubt, having hoped that at least one
elector would vote for “ the boss ” and thus save
their perfidy from being discovered. But, quite
unabashed, each scrutineer sent in a polite note
requesting a guinea for attendance. Did they
get it? The candidate smiled, and returned to
Sydney.

That tragi comedian, the evangelist, is
round again. Evangelista and clergy secretly
hate each other, despite all their demonstrations
of affection. Between the lines of an evangelist’s
speech, there can always be discerned a sneer at
the clergy, doing their puddle-horse mill routine
of work. The Evangelist, starring around, has
certain purple-patched orations, which he delivers
everywhere, and then people grumble because
their hard-wrought parson cannot speak the same
way all the year. So the parson says—to his
wife—that the evangelist is a humbug, after
which he has to go and crawl to the Rev. Pan-
jandrum.
» * •
Sydney Presbyterian thrift suffered a
shocking set-back at the hands of Talmage. What
Tabnage has in common with Presbyterianism is
not very clear, but, anyhow, the Presbyterians
were going to take him up and protect him from
the other denominations which yearned to have a
finger in the lion’s pie. So a grand ceremonial
reception was to be held in St. Stephen’s church,
and hundreds of free tickets were distributed to
Presbyterian pillars in every congregation. But
as soon as Talmage—or was it His Grace the Most
Rev. Bishop R. S. Sinythe?—heard how the cream
was to be taken off his half-crown lecture on the
following night, he gently, but firmly, intimated
that he could not appear. The rage of the intend-
ing deadheads was something awful. It is clear
that Talmage has a good deal in common with
Presbyterianism. lie hasn’t come to Australia
for the good of his health.
* # •
A sailor and his girl went to a Sydney
church to be married the other day. “ But you
haven’t brought a witness,” said the minister.
Well, they hadn’t thought of the witness. “Never
mind, my wife will be witness.” And she was.
When t'ne ceremony was over, the couple paid the
fee and turned to go. “Five shillings more,
please!” “What for?” “For my wife —she
always gets five shillings as witness.” “Well,
I’m !” said the sailor.
• # •
A Sydney suburban curate lately an-
nounced a lecture on “ Wit and Humour ” at the
local hall, and, as the shepherd had a poor salary
and was much respected, nearly everyone in the
parish bought a couple of tickets in order to make
the thing a pecuniary success. _ When the time
arrived, however, wit was not in evidence, as the
lecture never eventuated. Of humour grim
humour—there was plenty, for only one person
rolled up. All the others who had bought tickets
had reckoned upon someone else putting in an
appearance!

Lady Hopetoun’s mother, Lady Yentry,
was only recently presented at Court, her sole
purpose in rushing into this awful expense being
the presentation of a daughter, by herself, rmme-
diately afterwards. Papa Ventry, by the way,
has brought up a family of 16 on a modest income
of £7OO a year. Which explains a good many
things in Australian sassiety history.

Another society divorce-case is talked of
in Adelaide with a particularly pious doctor as
the co-respondent, but it is asserted that there is
documentary evidence of blackmail. Queen’s
Counsel are retained on both sides, and society is
expectant.

Gov. Hopetoun :—“ Ido not think God would have sent
us into the world if He did not intend us to be happy.”
When the wind blows through the crannies and
the rain falls through the roof,
And you find in baby faces looks of sorrow and
reproof;
When the cupboard’s gaping empty, and in pawn
your goods are stored,
Don’t forget the Lord’s intentions as delivered by
the lord.
When the rent is overdue, and you-are dunned for
the amount,
And the man who sells the firewood wants a trifle
on account,
And the wife has got rheumatics, and the doctor’s
got the huff,
Kick your heels and dance a breakdown —’tis a sin
to cut up rough.
Though you’ve tramped both town rnd country,
seeking work that none will give,
And you can’t escape the puzzle: “ How the deuce
are we to live ? ”
Ne’er allow your mind to linger on the rifle or the
rope.
But remember still the beautiful and joyous creed
of Hope.
He has palaces and riches, and his children never
know
What it is to starve and suffer, and the keen
winds never blow
On their unclad limbs in winter; who should
better know than he
How good is God—how happy He intended us to
be?
Perchance upon the fruitful earth the great
Jehovah meant
That His toiling, sinning children should have
plenty and content;
But if so the time is with us when the keen edge
of His sword
Should smite the class that trifles with the wishes
of the Lord.
E.D.
# * *
N. Queensland is fast reconverting Sunday
into the day of pleasure and gaiety which it used
to be in England before the Puritan revival.
People cannot very well bait native bears; but a
public football match between Charters Towers
and Mount Morgan was played on Sunday,
July 22. So successful was the sport that applica-
tion was made for a Sunday special train for the
return match ; but the Commissioners, in fear of
the Churches, had reluctantly to refuse.

By 22 votes to 20, S.A. Assembly has
decided that women shall not sit on Vermin
Boards. Howe, who pretends to be a Universal
Suffragist, but who is, above all, an Oppositionist,
described the Ministerial proposal as a travesty of
Adult Suffrage and an insult to Womanhood.
No lady, he thinks, should be allowed to watch
the skinning of a rabbit or a wild dog. Grainger,
another Radical, stigmatised the suggestion as a
foolish theft from that idiotic country Maoriland ;
and Caldwell, the original advocate for giving
votes to “females with property ” (cows with
bells round their necks and other animals of the
gender), would have crossed the floor but for the
mingled expostulation and main force of Premier
Kingston. There are fees attached to the Boa’ - d,

Dear Bulletin, —The champion mean man
has arrived. His wife invested her savings in fur-
niture and started a genteel boarding-house. She
replied regularly to advertisers in search of “com-
fortable home,” &c., and gave him the letters to
post, but never received any answers. He used to
tear up the letters and invest the stamps or money
in leer.— Wcrlie.
• • •
One Sydney bank was not represented at
the “bankers’ night,” at the Criterion Theatre,
last Thursday evening—whereby hangs a tale.
The decree which forbids this bank’s officials from
attending such functions, dates back to the halcyon
days of Mrs. Brown-Potter and Kyrle Bellew’s
season at Sydney Royal. On a certain memorable
night, during their season, a party of bank-clerks
engaged, for a stiff sum, the whole of the family-
circle and cleared a goodly amount by sub-letting
seats to fellow-clerks. Then the question arose —
vvliat to do with the balance? and one bright
genius proposed a banquet to the lovely
“Cora.’' Few who attended will forget that
banquet, and to this day many a bank-
clerk proudly exhibits a plate or a cup
or a saucer, on the back of which “ Cora ’
has scrawled some very tender sentiments. Each
banqueter took away some bit of crockery or other
on that occasion, and the caterer’s bill for broken
and missing china was something enormous.
When the revellers awoke to a sore head under
the table the enchanted spell rudely broke; the
lovely “ Cora ” had disappeared, and nothing in
particular remained but the sore head and the
lillets-doux on the cbinaware. Then each sadly
placed a bit of crockery under his coat and, in the
dim morning, silently stole away. When news
of the orgie reached a certain pompous general-
manager the fiat went forth that, in future, any
of the bank’s officials found taking part in any
similar functions would be instantly dismissed.
Consequently, Sydney “bankers’ theatre-parties”
are not what they used to be.

An unreported incident of the Melb.
theatrical football-match. One beauteous crea-
ture came to the conclusion that selling things
was fearfully slow business, so she charged half-a-
crown a turn for the privilege of lifting her on to
atable and jumping her off again. Many johnnies
indulged in this delectable exercise.
There is a proposal abroad to change the
name of Woolloomooloo. Now, Woolloomooloo
it the only place in Australia, besides Botany Bay,
that the average Englishman ever heard of. The
Britisher’s ignorance of any place outside his own
little foggy pimple in the North Sea is simply
stupendous. And now someone coolly proposes
to rob him right away of half of his limited geo-
granhical knowledge.

Cabled that a man has committed suicide
at the Strand Hotel, London ; identity unknown
at the time of writing. The interest appears to
lie in the fact that the “person” was “of gentle-

but for the invention of telegraphy we might never
have known of this sad occurrence.

Clean-shaven Captain Charlton, who took
command of the Austral after her long slumber at
the bottom of Sydney harbour, died in Shanghai
recently. He bore the reputation of being one of
the smartest mariners in the Orient Company’s
service, and he was certainly one of the strictest
disciplinarians that ever won the hand of a wealthy
lady passenger. For some time past the Captain
had acted as superintendent for the China Navi-
gation Company at Shanghai. #

football match, and tossing down half-a-sovereign
jauntily says: “Aw, Nellie, how-do? Faive

for calling me * Nellie ! ’ Good-day.”

The fowl at the steamer table was so tough
that he asked the steward to take it away. “It
isn’t the fowl’s fault, sir,” said that functionary—
“ the muscles of its legs become tough through the
vibration of the screw.”
# # •
Sydney Evening News publishes a letter
written by Cummings, the Barraba murderer, to
a N.S.W. country paper some time before Ins
crime, and deduces therefrom that if he hadn t
taken to murdering bank managers he would
“hive stood an excellent chance of .-getting into
Parliament as a labour representative.” “He
shows,” says the Noose, “an easy familiarity
with the phrases which are supposed to catch on
with popular audiences.” This, by the way,
is especially good as corning from a print which
has so many stereotyped newspaperese phrases on
hand about ‘‘constitutional procedure” and “the
legitimate investment of capital” and “sound
Government,” and the like. But the really in-
teresting feature of the letter is the writer’s ex-
pressions of his utter loathing for Premier Dibbs
who afterwards became his Nemesis and hanged
him. Probably Cummings had no premonition
when he wrote that Dibbs would ultimately hang
him, but the coincidence is grotesque all the same.
He also expresses an utter detestation of Parkes
and Reid, and if he had murdered his banker a
few weeks later one of them probably have
hanged him instead of Dibbs, which would have
been another coincidence. _ Cummings was ap-
parently a man of many possible coincidences.

A policeman at Melb. City Court the other
day was ordered to fetch the Koran, on which a
Mahommedan was to be sworn. The zealous and
intelligent officer, after shouting for “Mr.
Keoran” till he was hoarse, returned with the in-
formation that “ sich a person” was not in the
vicinity “av th’ coort.”

M‘Neill, the low-comedy evangelist per-
forming to large audiences in Melbourne, beseeches
his hearers to call out their names if they want to
be prayed for, or the names of any of their friends
who seem to need it. “My only wife” was the
order given by a forgiving pietist at one scramble
for grace.
# * •
A M.L. child recently died from brain
tubercle. “What causes brain-tubercle?” said
the father. “Maybe milk from a tuberculous
cow,” replied the doctor. Then the father went
to his milkman, and found a tuberculous cow.
THE UNIVERSAL PRAYER,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I ask Thee not, O gracious Lord,
For prize of power or rich award ;
I ask Thee not for tongue of flame
To fire my Muse and spread ray fame;
No laurel crown do I desire,
To no dim heights my hopes aspire;
Not mine the quenchless lust of gain,
Not mine the wish to rule and reign,
But here in peace and calm content
To Heaven my poor petition’s sent—
Give me, O Thou whom mortals bless
(For one small want I must confess),
Give me—to crown my .happiness—
A little more than I possess.
Queensland.
P. LUFTIC.

“ Heathens are people who don’t quarrel
over religion.”
Parkes has been bombarded with con-
gratulations on the attainment of his 79th birth-
day last Sunday. A Sydney D.T. interviewer
went with a blithesome, springy step to dive into
the old man’s recollections of infancy, and ex-
tracted much information regarding his capacity
for goo-goo utterance and mud-pie manufac-
ture. Singularly enough, however, the veteran
omitted the one thing material, and did not say
that he owes his strength and health, under Pro-
vidence, to a judicious use of Marshall s lonic
Nervine, sold at corner of Pitt and Park streets,
Sydney.
DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad Aide for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you? Will you do your business at

The “New Art,” as exemplified by “The
Yellow Book,” would seem to indicate that the
long-standing reason of the book-reading British
public has at last departed for good.
At a Melbourne police auction-sale of
unclaimed property, the other day, a dealer
bought, according to catalogue, “a handsome gold
watch ” for 355. Put to the test, it proved to be
brass. A refund was applied for and refused.
The Crown-Solicitor ruled, however, that it would
have to be granted, and it was.

Butter in the Melb. markets Bd. to Is. per
lb. A few seasons since the quotation was 2s. 6d.

felt locally. Likewise, machine-made bread is
being sold in Carlton at 3d. the 41b. loaf.

Once a crowd stood before Herbert’s
“Moses,” in Melb. Gallery. One critic was dis-
paraging, flouted Moses—artistically, and said
the mountains were bales of wool. An old lady
rushed and plucked the caretaker’s coat, with
“Do please come here. A man is uttering such
horrible blasphemies before that divine picture.”

a second honeymoon in the Blue Mountains.

Some years ago, a well-
known Sydney Police Court
practitioner and a high legal
luminary had arranged for a
day’s fishing at Manly. The
practitioner was most anxious
to get awa'y to catch his boat,
but had to prosecute some
dozen Italian fishermen for
fishing in close waters. The
swarthy offenders were ranged
side by side in the dock
“ How do they plead ?” asked
the Magistrate, and forthwith
a torrent of jargon proceeded
from each offender anxious t"
assert his innocence. “What’s
that they say ?" enquired the
Magistrate, also eager to g<>
fishing at once. “They all
plead guilty, your Worship,’
replied the alleged lawyer,
hurriedly putting on his ha*
and coat and looking at hit
watch. “Twenty shillings, or
seven days,” said the beak,
gathering up his quart of
prawns from beneath the seat
of justice, and amidst the
clamorous expostulations of
the Italians the legal gentle-
men rushed out of Court and
went fishing.
Mr. and Mrs. Grant, of Manly (N.S.W.),
celebrated their sixtieth wedding anniversary the
other day, and received congratulations from 190
descendants. This ancient couple married at
Roche-st. church, Limerick, 14th May, 1834,
when Mr. Grant was a sergeant in the 60th Rifles.

# • m
A London firm of publishers advertises a
new book about Thunderbolt, the bushranger, and
says that “the presence of an English lancer in
Bendigo as police officer is a guarantee that love
and sport find their proper place in this Austra-
lian tale.” But for the English lancer the Eng-
lish public would evidently have its doubts. The
last English lancer the writer ran across lived in
W. Queensland. He claimed to have been adju-
tant of a regiment, and chiefly passed his time
in fishing, while his harem of black-gins provided
’possum and sweet potatoes. (Love and sport, to
be sure !) He brewed a kind of spirit from his
sweet potatoes, and with that and tobacco cadged
from passing swagmen, he managed to get along.
Probably there are lots of similar English lancers
strewn over the provinces, helping to build up
Australian civilisation.

Fitz-Gibbon scorns the story that he wired
congratulations to York at the expense of Melb.
Board of Works. He sent the telegrams “on his
own.” Eitz.’s explanation proves that he regarded
the personal grovel as a promising investment,
and since doubts have arisen about the matter
Fitz. should send another telegram to the Duke
assuring him that Eitz., C.M.G., and not the
Board of Works, found the cash.

The Japanese Club (Torres Straits) num-
bers about 350 members. The nominal aims of
this club are admirable —the improvement morally
and socially of the resident Japs., the assisting of
needy countrymen, and the enforcing of a respect-
ful hearing towards their superiors —the Europeans.
The Jap., like the Chow, is always respectful in
the minority. “Unfortunately,” says a Northern
paper, “the society has no legal standing and
cannot enforce its edicts.” This comment is a

The sight of the Coolgardie Progress Com-
mittee turning out on camels to meet Premier
Forrest returning from Hannan’s was excellent,
writes a Bulletin correspondent. Some of them
then boarded a camel, for the first time, and so
the camels ambled about as they pleased, while
the riders clung on and shrieked. But all to no
purpose. The “Hooshdahs ”of the crowd made
the ship of the desert tuck in his flippers and flop
right down in the presence of the Progress Com-
mittee’s best girls.
Per Mariposa, there arrived at Sydney
from ’Frisco a young athlete, Mr. Charles Ramie 1,
a member of Liverpool (Amer.) Athletic Club,
who has undertaken, for a large wager, a walking
trip round the world, the journey to be accom-
plished in two years. The conditions are that 1m
shall carry no money, and shall not beg, borrow,
or steal. However, as he apparently is not bound
not to advertise, he's all right. Moral : Printer's
ink will help you round the world. Advertise in
The Bulletin, the only Australasian publication
vhich you will see everywhere, in every province.
Indigestion
may be the result of a variety of causes, debility
of the digestive tract, over mental work, nervous
prostration, excessive smoking, tea, or spirit
drinking. At any rate, no matter what the
•ause, the only treatment of any permanent benefit
is a restorative treatment, theremedy must possess
conic, invigorating and strengthening properties.
This is not the case with any of the many purging
mixtures sold as dyspepsia panaceas, and this is
proved by their makers stating that the dose must
be increased until the desired effect is attained.
This is due to the nostrum being purely a cathar-
tic, and by continued use the stomach, liver, and
bowels become educated to the action of the
medicine and it gradually loses all effect.
Clements Tonic is quite distinct in its action, it
assists digestion, promotes the flow of gastric
and pancreatic fluids, increases the secretion of
bile and thus assists the bowels to throw off the
waste matter; it strengthens the nerves, forms
bone, muscle and nerve tissue, and increases the
appetite. Clements Tonic is a natural restorative
of a debilitated system. Read the case of Mr.
William W. Brown, Inspector W. and S. Board,
Gowrie-street, Newtown, who writes on Dec. 28th,
1892 :—For about two years I have been a severe
sufferer from biliousness and indigestion, with
constant attacks of dizziness, in fact, I have been
at times incapacitated from following my daily
avocations as a Government Inspector. At times
when seized with dizziness I have had to sit down
to keep myself from falling. Hearing so much
about the nerve-strengthening qual i ties of Clements
Tonic, I resolved to give it a trial, which I have
done with the most beneficial results. I had only
taken two bottles when I found myself thoroughly
cured. lam only too happy to add my testimony
to the number already received-—Wishing you
every success, and a happy new year, I r< main,
yours gratefully, William W. Brown, Inspector
W. and S. Board, No. 5 Gowrie-Terrace, Gowrie-
street, Newtown. N.S.W.


The Australian Joint Stock Bank, Limited.
HALF-YEARLY MEETING OF PROPRIETORS, THURSDAY, 26th JULY, 1894.
Chairman’s Address.
Gentlemen,—l am glad to be able to oongratu-
ate the Shareholders on the strong position of
the Bank. The Directors have thought it de-
sirable to keep very much larger reserves than
seemed necessary in the past.
2. The reserves of Coin and Cash balances
amount to £1,258,206 6d., which more
than covers all moneys payable on demand, and
we have in London £241,100 invested in British
Government Consols and our own Government
Securities.
3. Further Inscribed Stock has been taken up
to the extent of £36,641, bringing the total up
to £385,464. F
The New Fixed Deposits on
31st December last amounted to £183,361 0 0
This has been reduced by re-
payment to the Queensland
Government of 130,000 0 0
being the amount lodged in
Treasury Notes for circulation
by the Bank, leaving £53,361 0 0
to which has been added new
Deposit Money received during
the past six months M ... 84,643 0 0
Making ... .£137,904 0 0
as stated in the Balance Sheet.
4. On 20th April, 1893, the
indebtedness to Depositors of
the old Bank was ’.. £8,498,589 16 11
The amount now to be repaid is 7,526,269 2 0
The difference being £972,320 14 11
The reduction has been made as follows :
We have Inscribed Stock,
chiefly arising from the conver-
sion of Fixed Deposit Receipts,
amounting to ... £385,464 2 7
and we have acquired from sales
of property, debts paid, &0.,
Fixed Deposit Receipts amount-
ing to ... 586,856 12 4
so that in a little over a year we
have thus provided ... ... £972,320 14 11
towards the debts due to the Depositors in the
old Bank, and including advances to customers on
the security of Fixed Deposits the amount exceeds
a million.
5. The amount of new Share Capital called up
is .£308.284;, and the sum received, including
Calls paid in advance, amounts to £332,810
155., and there are unpaid Calls amounting to
■£14:,920. Besides this, there are the Calls on
2500 shares not yet allotted, but this number is
1388 less than it was six months ago, and we expect
that all will be arranged for.
6. There has b;en a considerable reduction in
the expenses owing in a great measure to the
closing of a number of branches which ceased to
be profitable.
7. Considering the general shrinkage the busi-
ness of the Bank has been well maintained, and
could have been enlarged had satisfactory seeuri-
ties been offered. Great care is being exercised in
taking new business and in the conduct of the
affairs of the Bank generally.
8. The late panic and its consequences, as well
as the uncertainty with regard to the land laws,
have affected the value of all our securities, but
there is in most cases an ample margin, and as
regards those not fully covered it is believed that
the sum set aside to provide for the losses of the
old Bank will be found ample, a reasonable time
being allowed for realisation.
9. Interest on Fixed Deposit Money. We are
paying interest on the deposits of the old Bank at
the rate of 4£ per cent., but the present rate for
deposits for 12 months is 4 per cent., and there is
a prospect of a further reduction. The reduction
will probably lead to the withdrawal of some
deposits for investment in real estate or more
active business, and should help to increase the
value of landed property.
10. The community has not yet recovered from
the effects of the late crisis, and still suffers from
having to pay calls, the low price of stock, and
real estate being unsaleable, so that the general
position of the Colonies must have been very
sound to have stood the severe strain of the last
twelve months. We hope in the near future that
things will be much improved. Business is grad-
ually improving, and the new goldfields lately
discovered are finding employment for a great
many of our people.
The Report was adopted. F. Jago Smith, Esq.,
and R. J. King, Esq., were appointed Auditors
for the ensuing year, and a vote of thanks was
accorded to the Directors and Officers.
