Napoleon Bonaparte I,
EMPEROR OP THE FRENCH.
THE SECRET OF HIS SUCCESS.
How he Preserved his Nervous System.
NAPOLEON BONAPARTE, second son of
Charles Bonaparte and his wife, Letizia de
Rainolins, was born at Ajaccio, in Corsica, on the
15th of August, 1769. In the year 1779 h« entered
the Royal Military School of Brienne le Chateau.
There he remained till the autumn of 1784, when
he was transferred to the Military School of Paris,
according to the usual routine. He lost his father
in 1785, and the same year he was commissioned
a second lieutenant of artillery, and served as such
at Valence and other garrisons.
Napoleon attempted to obtain military power
in Corsica, but having failed, he fled to France,
where his career as a soldier and leader of men
became the most wonderful known to history, both
before and after that period. He was soon pro-
moted to chef de batallion, and commanded the
artillery at the siege of Toulon, where he highly
distinguished himself, and was the author of the
plan of attack which led to the fall of the place.
He was then promoted general of brigade.
On the fall of Robespierre, Napoleon was ordered
to take command of an infantry brigade in the
Army of the West. Thinking this beneath his
dignity, he refused to comply with the order, and
was discharged as a general. He was, however,
appointed second in command of the Army of the
Interior, Oct. 5, 1795, under Barras, and repelled
next day an attack of the sections of Paris.
Napoleon was appointed to the command of the
Army of Italy, Feb, 23, 1796; he married on
March 9, same year, Josephine Tascher de la
Pagorie, widow of General Yictome Alexandra
de Beauharnais, and left Paris immediately for
Italy. Napoleon’s great military genius flashed
forth in its full brilliancy in this his first
campaign. His power lay in the rapidity and
boldness of his decisions, and in the untiring
energy with which he carried them out, confound-
ing his enemies by the suddenness and lightning
rapidity of his blows, which never gave them time
to recover. He performed a very clever move, by
penetrating the point of junction of two strong
armies which were opposing the French army, the
one consisting of 20,000 Piedmontese under Colli,
the other of 38,000 Austrians, under Beaulieu, and
succeeded most wonderfully, although the French
army was only 36,000 strong.
From now his career was one continuation of
triumphal successes, until almost the whole of
Europe and other important parts of the world
was at his mercy. He soon became elected First
Consul for life, and in 1804 he assumed the title of
Emperor, and this was decreed by the Senate
shortly afterwards. The institutions which he
created, and which still remain, were the restored
church, the judicial system, the codes, the system
of Local Government, the University, the Bank
of France and the Legion of Honour. Space will
not permit to describe his unfortunate end, but
suffice it to say, that the wonderful nerve-power,
which he possessed and preserved to the last, was
absolutely due to a secret medical preparation
discovered in Corsica, by a medical relation of his,
and without which his nerves would have been
shattered long before he had reached his great
triumphs.
This great nerve invigorator is known as the
Napoleon Vigour Powder, and is now being intro-
duced into Australia, the sole Australian agents
being The Imperial Drug Company, 239 Eliza-
beth-street (near Bathurst-street), Sydney, who
will forward free pamphlets and all particulars on
application.
The Napoleon Vigor Powder is simply marvel-
lous for restoring a broken-down nervous system,
either brought on through early follies or other-
wise, and an excellent preventative against and
cure for nervousness, despondency, melancholia,
lost vitality and debility. The remedy is sold in
boxes at 6s., or in courses of 5 boxes at 25s. Double
course, 45s. posted. Pamphlets, remedies, &o.,
and all particulars sent strictly unobserved by the
agents, the Imperial Drug Co., 239 Elizabeth-street,
Sydney, to whom all cheques, Post Office money-
orders, postal notes, &c., should be made payable.

The NAPOLEON VIGOUR POWDERS are a
certain cure for all kinds of Nervousness, Debility and
Constitutional Weakness, Lost Vitality, Want of Vigour,

Development. Boxes, 65.; course of five boxes, 255.;

Bishop Smythe’s latest star, Parson Tal-
mage, from Amurrika, opened on Thursday at
Sydney Centenary Hall in the presence of a vast
and enthusiastic crowd, and with Smythe himself
perspiring profusely at the front door. About 7.45
a lady who had paid her admission-money fell down
dead on the premises, probably through excite-
ment at the prospect of seeing the great preacher,
and at 8 precisely a little, round, fat, oily shep-
herd trotted up to the rostrum on two brief
twinkling legs, and behind him stalked the gaunt,
black figure of the much-advertised parson, like a
headsman who had pawned his axe and meant to
execute the criminal with his feet. Talmage is
well-provided with feet. Also, he has a long,
spare, angular frame, and a hat and coat could be
hung upon the knobs of his spine. His face is
pallid, clean-shaven, and eager; his eye is
that of a man who sees threepence on
the horizon; and he has long arms which
look as if expressly built to reach out for
portable property. His style of elocution was
probably invented by himself and patented ; no
other man could elocute in the same fashion with-
out all his clothes flying asunder at the seams.
Its principal feature is its unexpectedness. The
preacher’s great drum-like voice rises with a cry
like the yell of a lost soul, just when there seems to
be no reason for it, and the effect vaguely suggests
that Talmage himself is doing the talking, and
that the yells are put in by an accomplice, who
always gets his oue at the wrong moment.
Generally the pastor ends an impressive sentence
on the very top of a howl, and then breaks off
short like a precipice, and there is a ravine of pro-
found silence before he starts again. If the occa-
sion is very impressive indeed, he throws up his
arms as he stops, and then he conveys a vague
idea that he is a runaway horse and has got in
front of himself to pull the animal up. Generally,
however, his whoops and his gesticulations and
his discourse all seem out of tune with each other,
and an idea arises that he has wound himself up
and set his long arms to fly out at a certain
moment, so that they have to fly out whether it is
the right moment or not.
• • •
Talmage’s accent is, as he would himself
describe it, something “tumble,” and his voice
suggests a corncrake in low circumstances. His
opening lecture dealt with “ The Bright Side of
Things,” and his philosophy is that this is the
best of all possible worlds, and the best of all pos-
sible epochs, and everything is right generally.
The sky is just blue enough, and the grass just
green enough, and the waters just clear enough,
and our legs are just long enough to reach the
floor, and therefore we ought to be content. He
is full of the glad optimism which goes with a big
salary, and a big bank-account, and a good dinner.
There was not, in all his lecture, one gleam of a
suggestion that the world contained such things as
cold, hunger, pain, or misery. His illustrations
all dealt with banks, and bankers, and people in
business, and affluence generally—except when now
and then he spoke unctuously in his hard voice
about beautiful girls, and then the little fat preacher
on the platform beside him threatened to go into
convulsions. In his own hard, unsympathetic
way, Talmage is humorous; his jokes are very,
very old, but he has the art of whirling his lank
arms as he utters them till somehow they sound
almost new. Also, in his own hoarse and shriek-
ful fashion, he is eloquent. Even in his humour,
however, his yearning for hell-fire can’t be alto-
gether restrained. “The Bright Side of Things ”
is illustrated by a garish, penny-dreadful account
of the burning of the Smithsonian Institute, in
which Talmage lets himself loose about the
devouring element and the “spirits of destruction
and calamity and w-o-o-o-o-o-o-o-o-o-o-O-OE,” in
a sudden yell and an abrupt stoppage which sug-
gests that he has fallen down and broken both his
knees. Then he winds up with an irrelevant bang
and roar about the end of the world —the black-
ness, the ashes, the combustion, the general burst-
ing-Up of all things and the falling of everything
in the bottomless black abyss of frost and forgot-
tenness —and retires. And The Bulletin’s im-
pression when he is gone is that Talmage is a hard,
keen, eloquent man of business, and absolutely the
best time-payment sewing-machine canvasser on
this earth. In fact, his preaching strongly sug-
gests that the Deity is a sewing-machine, and that
Talmage is pushing the sale of it for all be is
worth.
** * «
Bland Holt unfolded the last attraction of
his season, “A Woman’s Revenge,” by the late
Petti tt, at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. There
is no horse-race in this production, and it travels
entirely on its own artistic merits. Its most re-
markable feature is the persistent insolvency of
the leading characters. Sass, as aggressively per-
pendicular as ever, finds in the second act that
all his money is gone through the machinations of
two villains, Cosgrove and Baker. Bland Holt
and his wife have an interview as to their financial
situation, and discover that they are both penni-
less. Miss Henrietta Watson, as a cruelly
maligned wife, is presently found living in a lone-
some cottage on a heath, where she hears that her
fortune has been embezzled and she is stone-broke
as well as lost. Information also arrives that the
good old father of the second villain has been
cleaned but—the first villain, apparently, has no
father. And then it transpires that the two
villains are themselves both utterly insolvent, and
they proceed to tear each other to pieces in the
hope of finding some assets on the remains. Some
other features are the awful persistency with which
Cosgrove and Miss Harrie Ireland pursue Mis s
Watson for seven disastrous years, while Sass is
away concocting schemes for bending himself ; the
ominous fashion in which Baker, the lowering
Mephistopheles of the play, looms up round un-
expected corners ; the artistic fashion in which a
hangman’s rope is nearly being fixed round
Henrietta’s neck; and various other things.
• # #
Further, there is something dreadful in
the hungry fashion in which Baker always hap-
pens to be looking through the window while
Cosgrove is up to some wickedness inside. Baker,
in fact, is the one thoroughly artistic character in
the drama. His biggest chance arrives in the last
act, where he becomes suddenly gaunt and yellow
and old, partly through insomnia and partly
through meeting the corpse of Cosgrove in his
mind’s eye at every corner. And when he appears
in the witness-box to fix the aforesaid rope round
Miss Watson’s unspeakably beautiful white
neck, and can’t keep his feet and hands
still, and has perpetually to lick his dry
lips nervously with his dry tongue, he is
a whole sermon on the text that the wicked
lawyer shall be found out and suspended. Misses
Watson and Ireland, Edward Sass, Bland Holt,
Cosgrove, and a very successful phild - actress
described as Smith, keep up the even tenor of the
rest of the show, and it is satisfactory to note that
most of the good characters recover their lost
money towards the end,
• • #
The author has missed one great sensa-
tion. When the heroine is acquitted, and two
large policemen arrest Baker in court, that person
ought to be found dead in his chair dead of heart-
disease, and terror, and the constant recurrence
of Cosgrove’s dead body in his mind’s eye—and
when the officers seize him he should simply
crumble on to the floor, and lie there staring with
sightless eyes at the roof. That would be an
artistic ending; the present one isn’t.
*% * *
On Saturday next, Our Nellie, supported
by Wallace Brownlow, returns to Sydney and
makes an auspicious reappearance at the Lyceum
in “Paul Jones.” This circumstance is so vast
and so memorable that comment is unequal to the
occasion and language retires discomfited. There-
fore the bald announcement is left standing by
itself, as above. If any person thinks he can
make any remarks which are equal to such an
occasion he is requested to communicate with the
editor, enclosing .£1 as evidence of his bonafdes.
* * *
The blood-boltered nigger at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) is doing his last lap on his way home to
the Better Land, and on Saturday he gives place
to the “Lion’s Heart an entirely new produc-
tion and alleged to be something tremendous.
m* * *
Miss Hilda Spong, of whom The Bulletin
prints an excellent portrait, is winding up her
season at Sydney Criterion with two nights of
Pinero’s “ Money Spinner,” by arrangement with
B. and 8., and a brief spell of “Dr. Bill,” by
arrangement with Miss Myra Kemble. Then the
fair Hilda departs by special arrangement with
herself, and Garner’s comedy co. comes along.
Making due allowance for all the disadvantages
under which she laboured, her season has been a
success, and if one person connected with the co.
had fallen down a stair at the very beginning of
the season and retired for prolonged repairs, it
would have been a much greater success than it
was.
** * *
With “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” the
second B. and B. season at Melb. Princess’ made
a dashing start. A first-night audience’s recep-
tion of such a piece is not a certain index to
popular opinion, but the cheers that came after
curtain-fall on Saturday seemed to have the ring
of good money in them. Evidently the perform-
ance found many enthusiastic admirers, and they
may excite an interest in Pinero’s fine play among
people who, of late, refuse to go anywhere for
serious thought. The bulk of the Melb. populace
has a deplorable objection to being stirred by any
kind of revelation, however. “ Mrs. Tanqueray ”
recalls “The Profligate” to mind, for both plays
deal with the merciless vengeance of an immoral
past upon a repentant present, and indicate that
the author has an Ibsenic kink in his brain.
“The Profligate” was a false creation, a theatri-
cal sham, a sickly impostor, and Pinero practically
dedicated him to the Social Purity societies,
“ Mrs. Tanqueray,” on the other hand, is a real
victim of natural, though particular, circum-
stances. No part of the play strains probability
save the part where Mrs. Oortelyon talks offen-
sively to the second Mrs. T. of her intimacy with
the first Mrs. T., in the presence of the bride’s
husband and friends. That, surely, is a libel upon
sassiety manners.
• * «
Admitting the truth, force, and all-round
realism of the story, it is still difficult to under-
stand why Pinero should be credited with having
taught a great lesson in a specially bold and honest
way. The lesson he really does teach would have
no application to a “ gay ” woman who married a
bachelor, for instance. Exceptional circumstances
are created to fit a moral, whilst the fact that they
are exceptional is artistically concealed. Mrs.
Tanqueray’s trouble is a step-daughter. Without
this domestic encumbrance she would have got
along comfortably. When our old friend Arthur
Orton was fighting his impudent law-suit, two of
the titled contributors to his “Tichborne Fund”
w ere men who had married courtesans. One of these
wives from off the town bore children to her lord,
and died happy in their love. The other was the
second wife of an earl. Their marriage was a
success, the scorn of Mrs. Grundy mattered little
to them, and now the earl’s widow is devoting a
respectable old age to almsgiving and religious
pastimes. Her ooronetted Tanqueray had no
daughter to commit the extremely unlikely act of
betrothing herself to her stepmother’s former pro-
tector. These aristocratic precedents point straight
at the one weak spot in Pinero’s play, or, rather, in
its alleged lesson.
* * *
Mrs. Brough surprised everyone by her
performance of Paula, which has doubtless gained
much confidence and strength since she created it
(for Australia) in Sydney, three or four months
back. Sometimes she lost the character for a
moment, and allowed her old “goody-goody”
accent to assert itself in defiance of her naughty-
naughty character, but, taken for all in all, it was a
convincing performance and the actress held the
house. Titheradge and Miss Noble scored heavily
in the last act, and Cecil Ward’s inebriety, and
the veiled suggestiveness of his offer to stop and
keep Mrs. Tanqueray company, couldn’t well be
bettered. The cast, otherwise, was the same as in
Sydney, with one trifling exception, and the play
was elegantly staged, as a matter of course.
** * *
At the Alexandra they threaten to produce
a revised version of “ East Lynne,” with a land-
slip included, on Saturday night. The manage-
ment are cordially invited to call out little Willie,
and let the land slip into his shoes.
The revival of “George Barnwell” at
Melb. Royal seems to have been a Coppin inspira-
tion, rather than a Holloway idea. It was the
G.O.M-anager who seized snuffle by the forelock,
so to speak, and placed private boxes for Barnwell
at the Presbytery’s disposal. The wily Presbytery
refused to come forth and be crowned with shame.
Those boxes were bare, silent spots in a building
otherwise crowded to the roof on Saturday night.
George Barnwell, as many bishops and deacons
don’t know, was the typical Y.M.C.A. youth of
last century. He was beloved by his merchant-
master, and his master’s daughter, and the chief
clerk. They loaded him with precepts, and gave
him access to the cash. One day a tall, wicked
woman invited Barnwell to her house. He went
prepared to discuss Scripture with the mys-
terious lady, but she merely pinched bis leg and
made him stop to supper, and, worse than that,
“the author of hisruin” next persuaded Barnwell to
rob his worthy employer and assassinate his fond
uncle. Nunky is a transitory person in this tragic
history. He just comes on to enjoy his usual hour
of prayerful meditation in a wood, and Barnwell
cuts his soliloquy short. Somebody from the front
of the house would probably murder him if he
were allowed to meditate much longer. Then
Barnwell and Millwood (the wicked woman is
known only as Millwood) are captured and doomed
to die. Millwood prepares herself for Sheol in a
dungeon at the rear, whilst Barnwell repents, with
awful sincerity, in a front scene. The merchant
and his daughter and the pious clerk all come to
see their dear young friend off to execution, but
before he goes he implores the gallery to remember
George Barnwell, His last wish is to serve
as a shocking example, in which case he
feels that G.B. can hang happy. The Bulletin
erroneously stated that the curtain descended
upon a gallows tableaux. The noose and other
fatal accessories are left to the imagination. In
fact, a large portion of Barnwell’s career is left to
the imagination, but the bit on view is a riotously
solemn joke, and no parson can deny that it
preaches the true parsonic wor-rd. J. F. Oathoarb,
Miss Maud Williamson, and Julia Merton are
active in this Christian mission-work. Young
George Majeroni, although necessarily a diverting
hero, speaks his turgid lines with intelligence,
and promises to uphold the family reputation
anon.
• • •
Another aged drama, “A Dream at Sea,”
is bracketed with Barnwell. The dialogue con-
tains some cheerful wheezes that come out quite
fresh after a long retirement, and the broad
comedy passages (the play was written by the late
Buckstone, of spicy memory) are full of breadth.
The romantic plot is likewise a caution. Robert
Inman slumbers in a cave and dreams that Miss
Alice Deorwyn has been murdered. She was not
really murdered outright, although her friends
have buried her in the family vault. Inman, a
gallant ghoul, abstracts the alleged corpse and
plants it in the haunted cave, where he lights
charcoal and prepares to die beside his clammy
prize. The charcoal fumes somehow restore
Miss Alice to life, and Inman carries her out to
get fresh air just before the villagers arrest him
for the murder. He can’t confound them by pro-
ducing the corpse, because the maiden has walked
off in the meantime, bub she returns later, and a
certain Black Ralph, the Wrecker, confesses all.
“A Dream at Sea” is worthy of its place beside
“Barnwell.” The present writer hopes that the
same grand old double-bill may run for many
weeks, and shake his sides every evening till
further notice.

Loyal Heart.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Open wide my western window.
Let me hear the wild bush speak,
Let her soft breath cool the fever
That is burning brow and cheek ;
Surely, Jim, a grander sunset
Never lit the Tringa Creek !
I am riding, riding ever
To the realms of perfect rest;
I shall clash my golden stirrups
With the stirrups of the best—
Riding to a brighter sunset
Than is lighting all the west.
All my heart is lit with longing
For the golden days of yore.
And, though life was only toiling
At a master’s galley-oar,
I would gladly give me over
To the whip and chains once more.
I shall leave some friends behind me,
And, until their memory fails,
There are comrades who will miss me
When the sky to eastward pales,
And old Loyal Heart stands waiting
With his lean head on the rails.
How the great bush soothes my sorrow
With the wind-song that she sings—
Frank can have my old bush-saddle,
All the racing-gear and things,
Give young Jack the Swordfish filly,
Make him ride her with the “ rings.”
And Loyal Heart, old Loyal Heart—
O, generous friend and true !
By honest grip of sunbrowned hand,
I ask this boon of you—
To give him in the blue grass bends
The rest Jhat is his due.
But if the ringing cattle break
The mounted horsemen’s chain,
If the “brumby ” mob should best you
In the timber or the plain,
When your colts and fillies fail you
Saddle Loyal Heart again.
When the sand is over fetlock,
When the black mud lifts and clings,
When the younger steeds are tiring
And the raking rowel stings,
You’ll hold them safe on Loyal Heart
From the mulga to the wings.
And when the old horse pays the debt
That Nature asks of all,
You'll give him, like his master here,
No gorgeous funeral;
But let the red sun weep for him,
The shadows weave his pall.
Here, hold my hand, old comrade.
For the sun is sinking low ;
The vast wild bush is calling,
And I must mount and go
Beyond the flame-red ranges,
Beyond the sunset glow.
GLENROWAN.
“Ib was said that a woman should not
vote because she could not fight, but a woman
risked her life for her country every time a soldier
was born.”— Maloney, M.L.A. {Vic.), in support
of his Woman’s Suffrage Bill.
“The first man who says ‘this field is mine’
introduces into society the germ of all calamities.
The fruit belongs to all; the earth to no one.”—
Rousseau.
Italy presents great opportunities for a
Speight v. Syme case. There they have a debt of
200 millions on the railways, and a yearly loss of
eight millions on the working.
Most of the draught “ eucalyptus oil” sold
by chemists consists chiefly of turpentine, with
which nearly all so-called essential oils, such, for
instance, as oil of lavender, are now extensively
adulterated. Adulteration should mean.,gaol every
time.
The Solomon Islander is a humourist.
Recently the steamer Kurrara. visited the Solo-
mons and required assistance to load copra. Nob
an islander stirred, but one • heathen growled out
something about “ one pound-a day,” while another
bawled out “eight hours!” Verily the Social
Ideals are making headway.
“Strange! Higinbotham was never idolised
and worshipped as several other Australian poli-
ticians have been !”—Brisbane paper. True —and
this is why :
No puny parasitic plant
Strikes root in gritty earth ;
No cringing, crawling s3 r cophant
Loves men of genuine worth.
P.L.
Parkes, that man of loose eloquence and
hair, says that the cause of Constitutional Govern-
ment has again triumphed in N.S. W. Parkes has
got that phrase so much by heart that if he were
wrecked in mid-ocean, and spent three weeks on
a raft living on raw fish, and went mad and threw
all his clothes into the sea, and was at last picked
up, naked and encrusted with salt, and raving
wildly about his Stella, and had to be chained up
in a bunk and fed on beef-tea for a month, the
first thing he would say when he came to himself
would be that the great principle of Constitutional
Government had been once more vindicated. For
the fiftieth time or more The Bulletin rises to
ask Parkes, what, in the name of drivel, is the
blessed cause of Constitutional Government?
All the rage in Paris. Latest Parisian
preventative posted in sealed letter, 1/2 stamps.
National Drug Co., Russell-st., Melbourne.
Salmon, a new Vic. M.P. : “If women
should have a voice in the laws they have to obey,
then because the brute creatures are governed
they, too, should have a vote.” (! !) Frank
Madden, the latest, and perhaps most blatant and
hide-bound back-number Tory in the House, said
that if the wife of the man on strike had a vote,
and attempted to exercise it in opposition to the
will of her husband, he would promptly “ black
her eye.” Madden is evidently in favour of giving
a vote to the wife of Fatman, because his serene
adiposity never “smacks his missus in the eye.”
The Vic. electors should be proud of the man who
holds that his wife shouldn’t have a vote merely
because his horse and his dog, his ox and his ass
don’t go to the poll.
The latest information from Paraguay is
that Lane and his own little band of followers are
moving into Argentina in search of that Elysium
which is always afar off ; while fever and destitu-
tion and all manner of ills are settling down on the
remainder of the colony.

There is a terrace of houses in Lonsdale-
street West, Melb., which was built throughout
by one man, named White, in the forties. White
finished one house first, and occupying it, pro-
ceeded on the remainder, and, barring help in
raising rafters and joists, completed the lot with-
out assistance.


Business Announcements.
The Caligraph Writing Machine. j
&
mHE CaLIGRAPH embodies all the highest
I developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that tlrs platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that tin
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 16 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

An Italian steamer collided the other day
witli a Russian ironclad in the Black Sea, and the
ironclad straightway went down to a peaceful
bed among the ooze and the shme. This is the sort
of thing which the ironclad almost invariably
does on the smallest provocation; a collision
breaks up the average monitor like so much glass.
The loyal Australian politician who feels himself
becoming inflated over the miserable Naval Tri-
bute Squadron should make a note of this, and pub
the memo, somewhere where he will fall over it
every day.
There died recently in the Ararat (Vic.)
Lunatic Asylum a patient who outrivalled Ber-
trand in the length of his confinement. In the
early fifties Dr. Alfred Yates Carr was practising
medicine at Ballarat, and assisted in attendance
unon the wounded at the Eureka Stockade fight,
Dec ’54- In he became an inmate of the
Yarra Bend Lunatic Asylum, from which he
escaped several times. He was chief witness for
defendants in the libel case, Bowie, superintendent
of the Asylum, v. the Argus, and under cross-
examination proved quite a match for “Dick”
Ireland, then at the height of his power. So
astonished was Ireland that at the end of the trial,
which finished practically for the paper, Bowie
getting £lOO on a minor count, that counsel
declared to his friends that he believed in the
doctor’s sanity.
Wardlaw, eminenb London barrister, was
great on poisoning-defences. When Mrs. Fres-
singwold was introduced to him by her friends,
under the charge of thus doing her husband to
death, he thought, “ This little blonde is the per-
fect Lucrezia Borgia and Brinvilliers. Let’s see
how she will lie.” But she didn’t. _ All she
wanted was to die. She had been deceived by a
villain, who bolted. Wardlaw got her off with
the finest speech on record. He married her.
The tension became severe, in a thousand imagin
able ways. They loved each other, but the rift
curie, entirely without her fault. He fenced her
round all he could. She had to discharge a ser-
vant, who said then, tartly, “Do you think I’m
going to stop here to be poisoned?” A worse
shock was the occurrence of a criminal trial, pre-
cisely similar to her own. Of course, Wardlaw,
the great expert, could not be in it. His mind
got out of hinge. He went morbidising, till he
fancied he was being slowly poisoned. At the
dinner-table, he shook up his coffee, in the saucer,
and said, “ I believe this is what you used for
him.” She burst into tears. He quite lost
control, and brutally asked her lover’s name—a
matter they had never referred to. She got up
and walked out of the room, straight to her bed-
chamber. In the morning she was found dead.
One arm hung out of bed, and the forefinger
seemed to point to a little bottle. __

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Owing principally to the refusal of the Nor-
woods to accompany the S.A. football team to Victoria,
the match turned out a miserable farce, the Melbournians
kicking 13 goals to 0 without exerting themselves.
• * * *
The record mile walk of Corporal Clarke, of
S. A. Militia, which lowered the best previous time (6 min,
23 sec.) by 36 4-sth sec., was performed under adverse
weather conditions. Clarke stands somewhere about 6ft.
Bin., and claims to have a five-foot stride. Allegedly
competent judges assert that Clarke runs, but the writer,
who was there and is practically acquainted with the sub-
ject, having walked the mile in 30 min. 59 sec., is quite
satisfied as to Clarke’s bona fldes.
.** ♦ •
Sporting pressmen are considered to have a
claim on the handicapper’s billet. Perhaps this explains
why we hear so much about the necessity of appointing
an assistant to A.J.O. 0.0. M. Scarr.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s prediction that a galloway
would some day be returned as winner of Melb. Cup is
more likely than ever to be fulfilled. The Associated
Pony Clubs have decided to raise the standard from 14
hands Sin. to 15 hands. Many Sydney trainers could with
time get Pans to pass under that. In England the
standard is 15 hands.
♦ * * *
For Caulfield Grand National Hurdle Race next
month, Navarino, lOst. 81b., is reported to be “not too
bad.”
* • * *
Startling rumours of bribery in re the defeat of
the Vic. Totalisator Bill. A leading bookmaker asserts,
from personal knowledge, that £7OO was got rid of by the
Hebrew bookies. It is certainly curious that many mem-
bers who opposed the Bill before, and who were known to
be about the House, did not vote.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
Capstan, a very powerfully-built son of Sheet
Anchor, may win the Caulfield or Melbourne Cup—
perhaps both. A real good horse to look at, and fast
enough to run fourth in last Newmarket Handicap.
The brother of one of Melb.’s leading bookies
nas just sent in his checks, leaving nearly five figures
behind him. Some distant poverty-stricken relatives
pome in for nearly the whole, to the consternation of
immediate connections.
* * • •
Brewer was to have ridden Daimio in the G.N.
iteeple, but was asked to stand down at the last moment,
owing, it is said, to his having had a “ go ” on the track
on his own hook, contrary to orders.
• • * •
Bloomer, the N.S.W. dog which won the last
Waterloo Cup, killed five out of six hares in the
trials. Both the Benalla and Moonee Valley piumptons
have hares which have been christened “Carbine.” The
Moonee Valley Carbine has for five or six seasons beaten
the dogs in the matter of speed, and is still alive. He is
generally reserved for the final course. It was said that
Bloomer killed the Benalla Carbine when on her way
over, but the secretary says this is incorrect, and he offers
to lay 10 to 1 that the hare, which, by the way, has been
unsexed, will beat any two dogs in Australia.
* * • *
A somewhat boozy Victorian caused a lot of fun
f mongst the knowing ones the other Saturday and Mon-
day, by asking every bookie he met “ Where did Giblets
run in the Canterbury Handicap ?” A wag had sent him
a wire on Saturday from Brisbane, signed “Adams,”
informing him that his ticket had drawn Giblets for the
race named.
• ♦ * •
Extraordinary odds were landed by the “working
miner ” at Korrumburra who won* the pigeon-shooting
match the other day. His left arm was in a sling, and
his friends loudly proclaimed that shooting would do the
patient harm. When the commission had been worked,
the miner unslung his arm and potted his birds without a
miss. The local books, after being skinned to a man,
were informed that the winner had seen better times, and
would then have been backed for thousands, but could
find no one to take him on.
* * * •
Thestarting-maohine was a success at the Cante-
rbury Park (N.S.W.) races on Saturday last. It has come
to stay. Lady Ida started a warm favourite for the
Hurdle Race, Underwood having crossed the border
specially for the mount; hut it did not come off, as
Highborn 11., who ran so well at Warwick Farm, beat her
by a length.
The Chester colt, Duke of York, won the Flying
Handicap, and one of Sam Hordern’s importations—a
three-year-old named Isolena, by Doubloon, My Lena-
won the Park Stakes.
The Canterbury Handicap fell to E.K., who
heat Whist by a couple of lengths. Whist started un-
backed, and with 71b. over-weight, so his running must
have proved rather a surprise to his party.
• • * *
An immense crowd filled Sydney Cricket
Ground on the occasion of the return Rugby match,
N.S.W. v. Queensland. The play was not as keen as
might have been expected from the previous Saturday’s
match, as N.S.W. fairly ran over the Bananalanders.
White was again the hero of the day ; but Warbrick did
not play with his usual brilliancy for Queensland, no
doubt from the fact of an injured shoulder, and the
respect with which he treated White, who had handled
him so roughly the previous Saturday.
* » * *
Torpedo, the horse in which pugilist Tom Leea
held an interest, and which was rather a sensational animal
in Sydney some time back, won the July Handicap in
Adelaide on Saturday. At same meeting, Port Admiral
got the father of a dressing from Lord Granville in Tatter-
sail’s Handicap.
* * * *
At Oaklands (Vio.) Hunt Club Meeting, Dr.
Cortis’ Jacob, with the reasonable weight of 9st. 131bs.
and-Mr. H. Cortis in the saddle, won the miie-and-half
Open Hurdle race.

A Divorce Court Wrinkle.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The wife had long been engaged in a
secret amour. Occasion had frequently been
safely kind. But there came one disastrous even-
ing when the husband, returning home unex-
pectedly, “learned all.” After the inevitable
storm, he promptly interviewed a lawyer, in order
to institute proceedings for divorce.
“Have you a photograph of your wife?’’en-
quired the man of law.
“Yes.”
“ Bring it to me.”
The husband complied with what seemed to him
a strange and irrelevant request.
“ H’m ! You’ll never succeed against so pretty
a woman as she is. Now, take my advice. Go
and see your wife, or bring her to me, and fix up
an agreement that she shall apply for a divorce
against you.”
“Against me ? But she can’t; I’ve not erred
against her in the least matter.”
“ Never mind. We’ll manufacture the grounds
—desertion and cruelty, adultery, anything.”
“ But I don’t follow the scheme.”
“Be guided by me. If you prosecute her, you’ll
fail, as you have no witnesses. She will probably
apply for a separation and alimony. If she
prosecutes you, we will have an agreement pre-
arranged that, if you don’t defend the case, she
will not apply for alimony. Then you will be quit
of her, and that’s your object, I suppose?”
“Yes.”
And it was so settled.
» • •
A charming lady, soberly but tastefully
costumed, tells her terrible domestic troubles in
Court. An emotional Judge keeps sopping his
handkerchief to his eyes, as he jots down his
notes on the case, and cries occasionally ; “Stop a
minute, please, madame,” because his feelings
clog his caligraphy, and he must needs pause to
shove up his spectacles and mop away his tears.
There is no appearance of the respondent, and
the name of the co-respondent is unknown.
His Honor : “ I see how it is, my poor woman.
You have been most brutally and shamefully
treated by your husband. The circumstances
related by you, together with your demeanour in
Court, clearly reveal to my experienced and
trained judgment that your own conduct has been
irreproachable. How is it that the most angelic
women generally choose as husbands the most de-
praved men? I grant a rule nisi, returnable in
three months.”
And all parties are satisfied.
* * •
The above is a fact, and fresher than some
“new-laid ” eggs. Further, a barrister informed
me that in no less than five divorce cases in which
he was engaged during one week, a similar plan of
action was followed—with “ satisfactory ” results,
and no application for alimony. __
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
A new S.A. industry is the mouse-liar.
From S.A. Register:—
A farmer near Muloowurtie has to fight the mice from
his plate while having his meals. At Petersville a cat was
put into a room where the chaff was raised from the floor
and protected by tins. On opening the door the farmer
found millions of mice on the ground and the cat with its
tail erect, evidently frightened out of its wits. Directly
a light was shown the mice scampered off, and the oat
disappeared and has never been heard of since.
Sausages in some S.A. country districts are now
only Id. per lb.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
William Bates, of South Melbourne, took
a pair of boots from a shop, the other day, and
straightway departed with his plunder in quest of
a policeman. Having accomplished the act of
larceny his next move was to get himself arrested
and committed to prison for as long as possible.
As an honest man Bates had proved a failure; he
could 'get no work, the pangs of hunger were
troubling him inside, and this bold stroke of policy
was his last resource.
In 1782 that gifted fossil, poet Oowpen
wrote: —
The Frenchman, easy, debonair, and brisk,
Give him his lass, his fiddle, and his frisk,
Is always happy, reign whoever may,
And laughs the sense of misery far away:
He drinks his simple beverage with a gust,
And, feeding on an onion and a crust,
We never feel the alacrity and joy
With which he shouts and carols, Yive le Roy t
Filled with as much true merriment and glee
As if he heard his king say—“ Slave be free 1”
A few years later the debonair Frenchman carolled
the “ Marseillaise ” and guillotined his Roy.
Don’t be rough on William Bates,
Don’t be hard on Billy ;
Safe within the prison gates
One is sure of “skilly.”
Lock him up and keep him good.
Guard him from his neighbour;
All he asks is simple food
For any kind of labour.
Let him do “ a bit of time ”
Decent-like and proper,
Sans the option of a crime
Pleaded to a “ copper.”
Not for money William sought
Boots that wasn’t his’n ;
Theft was but his happy though*
For getting into prison.
Felons’ fare is not a feast
Tempting to the wealthy;
Bates would say that, at the leasts
Hominy is healthy.
Bid him linger on awhile
Grateful for his ration ;
Bliss for Bill is durance vile—
Freedom spells Starvation.

"A Yellow Aster.”
A new novel may be judged in two aspects— as a
work of genius, or as a work of talent. You may
pit it against the great fictional creations, niched j
in history, consecrated by tradition, supreme as
art; or you may compare it with current books to
amuse, and gauge success by its vogue, its in-
terest, and its fitness to the mood of the moment.
In the one view “ A Yellow Aster ” has no stand-
ing ;in the other, it is a book of high merit. It
is undeniably clever, though the cleverness is only
superficial; it has power, but no reserve power.
You feel that the authors have done the best of
which they are capable, and recognise that the re-
sult is enjoyable without being admirable. Com-
pared with “The Heavenly Twins ’’and its satellites
the superiority of “A Yellow Aster’’ is easily
apparent. It is not morbid, it is not melancholy ;
it is coherent and complete. Special interest for
Australian readers lies in the fact that Dr. and
Mrs. Caffyn were formerly residents of Mel-
bourne ; for Bulletin readers, in the fact that
Dr. Caffyn frequently contributed to these
columns. Though the whole credit of authorship
is yielded to the lady, it is presumed that her
husband must take praise for a good deal of the
plan, and for most of the method and
arrangement, as well as for the added strength
given by those touches with which it is com-
paratively easy for a revising collaborator to im-
prove and illustrate the thought of his partner.
This revision has been ably done, with the result
of leaving nothing that is weak, and little that is
unnecessary, so that the book gains steadily in
interest to the climax.
# * •
Given the plot, everything goes of itself. A
queer, intellectual, out-of-the-world father and
mother “produce” two children—one a boy, an
excrescence on the story; the other a girl, its
heroine. The parents come out of their studies —
brown, well-furnished, and various—to direct
that their offspring shall be taught everything
but religion. This, however, is only a side-issue,
and its influence on the main theme is inartisti-
cally suppressed later on. But the students have
also no time to love their children, and the central
picture is that of the girl, thrown back on herself,
with her affections stifled, growing up to dormant,
sexless womanhood. She marries as an experi-
ment ; and the rest is a description of her heart-
awakening—first through her mother's death,
afterwards by the birth and sickness of her child.
Then her husband, who has had a rough time of
it, is repaid for his rash adventure.
# * •
The book is mainly dialogue, and the dialogue,
is mainly entertaining. Gwen (the heroine) thinks
the force ruling the world is quite human in its
temper and its injustice.
“Rather capricious and old-womanish, too —1 often
think that.”
“Why?”
“Why! From the stupid times and places that earth-
quakes and waterspouts and things come ; they hardly
ever burst up or beat down desert islands or places like
that; they always flock to populated places where people
have been working for years to make themselves comfort-
able, and then all in a minute their work is undone, and
they may think themselves lucky if they aren’t undone
themselves. That sort of thing seems reasonless and like
an old man.”
The heartless Gwen wishes she could flirt like
other girls ;
It looks such a delightful pastime 1 I have seen plain
girls look positively quite beautiful when engaged in it.
The undercurrent of heaps of girls’ lives, upon which it
seems to me all the rest is built up, is a sort of simmer-
ing, unconfessed, vague longing for the sensation of being
“ caught and kissed,” like the little brown maid in the
old rhyme ; not in a general vulgar way, but in a well-
bred particular way. It is a quite incomprehensible
sensation to me.
That is wicked ; but this is worse ;
Even virtue in women is very much a matter of tem-
perament, and where the temperament is, there will the
opportunities be gathered together.
Not very original, maybe; and, indeed, striking
originality of thought is not the authors’ strong
point. But their book is a very pleasant and
satisfactory evening’s reading.

A Study of Revenge.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
, , . . , , ,
To say that at one time he was my dearest
friend is superfluous—for how otherwise could I
have conceived for him such an intense hatred;
how nourished such a consuming desire for
revenge ? ... , . . .
evenge was, indeed, my sole remaining passion,
At the very sight of him it burned with
dangerous violence; when the blood, boiling and
singing through my veins, threatened, no longer
abiding its accustomed course, to burst forth from
the facial orifices in scarlet suffusion.
Restrain yourself I—l reasoned; lest the very
excess of your passion defeat its gratification.
More especially did the fear of doing him a
violence cause me to exercise over myself a rigid
control. I or such was not the measure of my
vengeance. .
Should I in one furious moment put him for
ever beyond my reach ?_ Could a single draught of
his hot and fresh-flowing blood quench my im-
mortal hate ? If I had been guilty of such weak-
ness, which, for the fleeting ecstasy of a moment,
sacrificed the happiness of eternity, I would have
incontinently, with suicidal hand, reft my soul
from its vesture, to seek his—aye. it might be
beyond the very depths of perdition.
Time, which effaces all things, has not less-
ened the injury he did me ; for memory, with
its indelible pencil ever deepening the same lines,
still, as my soul lives, presents to me the eternal
picture of ray wrong. .
E r i ! gh T ! J? ee /> B * Y^ m Z e
it that I a e e I* 1 , T ririaai h]fi I dn
possib e revenge. And, when I say possible I do
not allow myself to be bound by the limits
of the conception or execution of any act of
vengeance since the world began I scorn such
a puny confine. ly revenge ra ■
any which had till then been conceived by mere
man. Years had intervened. During the whole
time I had worked with unceasing energy in
the furtherance of the object of my life.
I bewildered the brilliancy of his intuition,
which told him that “ never can true reconcile-
ment grow where wounds of deadly hate have
pierced so deep and we were the closest of close
friends. I endured from him petulance and insults
interminable. I rejoiced in them.
There were moments when I revelled, in
imagination, in the deep and exceeding happiness
which the future promised—a moment of bliss
prolonged to an eternity.
For some considerable time there had been en-
gendered within my friend—as he thought, of his
own choice; but, in reality, of my initiative—a
craving for metaphysical and psychological know-
ledge.
To this he entirely abandoned himself with a
steady determination and a feverish haste, as
though some great thing depended on the result of
his research. Nor would he be satisfied, until
he should stand on some remote and perilous
verge and peer into the unknowable.
Possessing, in a high degree, the divine
gift of imagination, he was also eminently
endowed with the mathematical and analytical
faculties—a rare combination. The exact sciences
were to him as the alphabet; and his conclusions
were invariably rapid and certain : it was only by
positive effort that he could form a false one.
The just solution to him was always the line of
thought of least resistance. His delicately-
balanced mind instantaneously separated the false
from the true.
By a taoit understanding between us, on these
subjects alone were our lengthy conversations and
arguments. He eagerly sought for, and as quickly
exhausted, the most erudite works on his favourite
studies. In all matters I appeared to him per-
fectly frank, except only as to certain philosophic
theories, wherein I seemed to exercise a sort of
reserve. I demonstrated the probability, showed
the absurdity, proved the possibility—but here
stopped. He plainly believed I had proceeded a
step beyond him in the achievement of for-
bidden knowledge. I held an ascendancy abso-
lute> bub pure i y men tal. At length the oppor-
tunity and the time converged to the accompiish-
menb G f my purpose.
W e were encamped at an immense distance
from human habitation, ostensibly for sporting
purposes. By an apparent mischance our books
b ad been left behind. The tent was pitched, at
the end and the bottom of an enormous rift in the
earth. This opening was in form and appearance
as though a gigantic thunderbolt had rent a moun-
tain. On three sides we were surrounded by its
towering and almost perpendicular walls, and on
the fourth was spread before us the desolation of
the plain. Its barren and blasted ground was
strewn with innumerable boulders of a glaring
white colour—the traces of some mighty and long-
passed glacier. The spot had been chosen in our
wanderings because of its almost weird beauty,
but it was a beauty that oppressed without
charming. A likeness to the abode of the dead
was too apparent in its tomb-like stones, some of
which, by chance poised on end, seemed to denote
a fresh interment.
For some days my friend had been in
strange unrest. His was a step that incessantly
refcur » ed to the e ncampraent-an eye that ever
tioni 80ught panting and fierce
% fcQ | now s what and w hy, I concealed. So
gtronß was this desire that it emanated from him
like some hypnotic influence to wrest my secret
f me-but stronger far was my resistance,
_„. * * ,
. On that evening the sun was setting strangely—
* was already declined beyond our vision, bub a
small blot of deep red colour covered its parting
and as the world turned over this gradually
increased in size and changed to a darker and
more purple hue, till it seemed a foul blotch ot
clotting blood on the otherwise stainless sky.
We we , re seat ed outside the tent, and be-
tween us burnt the camp-fire,
The bush sleeps during the day, beneath the
narcotic rays of the sun, but as night draws on
it wakes with its many voices. This night it
awoke, but remained in fearful and trembling
silence—only now and again was heard a slight
rustle or movement, as something living sought
a more obscure retreat. Perhaps this stillness was
because of the oppressive and stifling weight of
the air; or it might be the inhabitants of the
bush feared that dark, livid, and ever-increasing
mass which extended on the horizon and piled it-
self towards the zenith.
We had sab in silence for an hour. Not that
this alone was unusual; for in the Australian bush
solitude and silence beget a love of their like; bub
this silence was strange because of the pallor of his
face and the quaking of his frame, whilst the
quick and heavy thumping of my heart told of
an ecstatic excitement close at hand,
At length he raised his head, and with an
appealing look, called me by name.
“ Listen 1 ” I abruptly articulated, in a voice as
unlike my own as the words which now broke
from my parching lips.
As I continued, he gradually sunk forward on
his knees, and approached himself towards me,
inclining his head slightly, so as not to_ lose a
single syllable of my utterance. I hurried that
which I had to say, for I feared the interruption
of the storm within me, not less than of the now
fast approaching and terrific tempest, whose shriek-
ing and howling were heard in the distance.
He had crawled forward on the burning embers,
the light from which, flickering dimly, fell across
his face, whereon was mirrored the effect of my
words. His attitude was that of a child who fears
punishment. One hand was raised as though to
ward off a blow, whilst the other rested on the
still glowing remnants of the fire. A tiny spiral
of smoke arose from the scorching flesh.
As I spoke the last words, his face began to
assume the expression for which I had waited.
The black sky split, and a flood of dazzling blue
light surrounded us. At the same moment he
sprang upward.
“ I am then ” he exclaimed.
“Mad!” I shouted furiously, while the blood
sprang from my mouth as though to attest my
words. “ Mad !—and for ever ! ”
In another moment, I was in the saddle of my
waiting horse and had lashed it to a gallop.
As with maddened bounds the terrified animal
fled up the narrow path which skirted the very
edge of the ravine, I fancied I could distinguish
amongst the confusion of sounds one—not of the
elements.
I leaned out of the saddle, till the whole upper
portion of my body hung sheer over the yawning
precipice.
Then rose into my ears, from the foot of the
rooky wall, hundreds of feet below, despite the
roar and havoc of the terrible storm, which now
raged in its fullest fury—a prolonged and mourn-
ful cry. It was the voice of a soul from which
Hope had for ever fled.
An ineffable serenity pervaded my whole being.

A. LEGEND OF LOMBOK.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Upon a black sand beach, a cinnamon-
coloured man, with blue loin-cloth, yellow jacket,
scarlet head-kerchief. Conspicuous, surely; yet
of this polychromatic arrangement Morgan saw
nothing until it planted itself within two feet of
his nose.
The sand was black because it was volcanic;
the man was cinnamon because he was a Sassak;
and Morgan was preoccupied because he was in
an uncommonly “tight place.” Figuratively,
that is; for, literally, he was in a notoriously
loose place —Ampanat, in the island of Lombok.
Loose, at least, as regards the “dominant class” —
Brahminical and alien; though morally tight
enough with the Sassaks —Mahommedan and
aboriginal. But the Sassak, no more than any-
body else, considers himself bound to undertake
the morality of other people, nor did this parti-
coloured person’s profession differ very violently
from that upon which “Sir Pandarus of Troy ”
has conferred the lustre of his name. With grace-
ful salutations of the Orient, he professed, in
deferential accents and the Malay tongue, his
consuming eagerness to help the “ exalted white
master ” out of his present difficulty.
Morgan stopped booting the beach and laid a
sudden hand upon the yellow cotton jacket. “ My
difficulty? What is my difficulty ? If you don’t
happen to know what you’re talking about, my
leather-coloured friend, I’ll teach you to come
messing about Billy Morgan when he’s down-by-
the-head.”
Ju Somal understood only that he was being
threatened. He brought his kris-handle into
view, smiled an indulgent smile, and waited till
Morgan had repeated himself— minus the mination
—in Malay.
The Sassak stretched a lanky arm towards
where, beyond the snowy surf-ridges, the purple
of the Indian Ocean vanished into the dazzle
struck up from the inner anchorage by the in-
tolerable sun. “ There was your ship this morn-
ing. She is gone !—and you are here ! No friends !
No money ! Say I good ?”
In ten thousand sailor-men there may be one
who doesn’t swear. Morgan was that one, but he
was sorely tempted. “ Good ?” he muttered. “ It’s
bad ! —d-deplorably bad ! Can’t say the old man
didn’t warn me; but I never thought he’d have
done it!”
Now, the “old man”—which is to say Sandy
Graham, master-mariner and part owner of the
barque Jerusalem —hadn’t “done it,” after all.
Instead of being on his way to Amoy with his
cargo of trepang and sandalwood, the wily Sandy
had only stood away for Bali Strait, and was
waiting, in a manner, “ round the corner ” until
the lesson should have well bitten into his truant
second-mate.
“ We’ll slip roon’ again, the nicht,” he said to
his “first,” as he stumped up and down his little
slice of poop. “ He’ll scarce believe his een whan
he sees the auld craift at her ainchor again in the
mornin’! A’m theenkin’ we’ll cure the laddie,
this turn, o’ dilly-dallyin’ ashore wi’ the lasses
whan the vassel’s hove-short! ”
• # *
Ju Somal explained. Simi-Lik had sent
him.
“ The Chinky girl! ” thought Morgan. “ Pooh
—what can she do ? ” Which was scarcely fair to
the canary-coloured damsel whose mercantile
parent had so hospitably entreated him, and who
was the more sorry for his predicament in that
she considered her own fascinations as partly the
cause thereof. The sailor, at all events, had hardly
got out of Pah-Pak-Wak’s house before that
eminent citizen entered it with the news that the
Jerusalem had sailed. Simi-Lik, in tribulation,
had sought her Sassak handmaiden, who had
sought Ju Somal, who had sought—and found —
the disconsolate Mr. William Morgan.
“A thankless kind of a beast!” was what the
sailor called himself when, by Ju Somal, all this
had been duly recounted. “ Real good-hearted
little girl, it must be; even if it is a bit greenery-
yallery ! Off you go, spindle-shanks, and say I’ll
come !”
“ Spindle-shanks ” understood, and was off, top-
speed, to claim his promised dollar. But, as he
ran, he ruminated. A dollar ? Why, there were
two or three dollars in it! Bah ! Jankan was
rich ! He would surely give five dollars to be told
of this thing! And to Jankan’s house the brown
shanks flew.
# • »
Morgan sat under the mangoes and won-
dered. This was what he had sneered at as the
“Chinky girl”—this laughing, lovely, splendid-
eyed creature, with a flowery wonder in its glossy
hair, and silken miracles about its graceful shape.
In Malay—the lingua franca of the Archipelago—
she chattered gaily to the white man. Her father
was very rich, and very fond of her. He would
do anything to please her. Also, he was fond of
white men—and so was she ! And slyly she
watched Morgan over her gold-and-ebony fan.
He warmed to the idea. There was money in
it—lots of money! And the girl was fit for an
emperor ! But then—a Chinky wife ! Frightened
by a word, he froze, and coarsely told a woman,
who—race for race—was vastly better bred than
himself, that he “wasn’t on.” The words were
Malay, but their value was about the same, and
the “woman scorned,” looking him steadily in
the eyes, mentally sentenced him to death!
» • •
Pah-Pak-Wak, known to the few Britishers
in Lombok as “ Papa Quack,” was of a personality
inconceivable by those who have seen only the
“ scrubber ” Chinaman of Australia and the
States. Six feet and 16 stone, with strong, saga-
cious countenance and snowy cone of beard —gold-
rimmed glasses sat on his respected nose, a gold-
linked white silk blouse enveloped his portentous
stomach, blue cashmere trousers cased his sub-
stantial legs, and a red silk tuft completed his un-
speakable pigtail.
To him quoth Jankan, “ Have a care. I can do
much with the Raja! He will not see me
wronged.”
Pah-pak put down his chopsticks and smiled—
the immemorial Celestial smile. He —which is to
say, his dollars —could do more with Lombok’s
ferocious ruler than any other man in the island.
But, despising—brains and body—this skinny little
Indo-Malay, the man of business wasn’t going to
argue the question.
“It is of my daughter we speak, not of the
Raja. It is but common kindness Simi-Lik has
showed this English stranger. But, in any case,
she shall please herself. We make not slaves of
our women, like .”
“Like the men of Lombok 1” Jankan swelled
his five-feet-nothing to another inch or so, came a
step nearer, and slowly drew forth his kris.
“ Seest thou this ?” he said.
Pah-Pak coolly reached out for the weapon,
examined the gold inlaid hilt, passed his finger
oyer the ridgy steel of the blade, and handed it in-
differently back. “ Pretty fair !” he said, and took
up his chopsticks again. “But I don’t want to
buy-”
Jankan turned green—Malays do turn green—
at the insulting assumption that he wanted to sell,
and his hand trembled till the kris clattered
against its wooden scabbard as he sheathed it.
“ Dog !’’ was all his colourless lips could utter.
Pah :. l>ak ~ h ? was eating dragon-flies fU’AMi?e—
carefully fished out one of them, balanced it over
the dish upon a chopstick, and looked significantly
nn.iil-fT at, gre M y gr T msect and th ® \ en T'
ous little Malay. No words were wanted for his
meaning : he dreaded the one about as much as
th Bnh h !f‘ft. he in, . „ ~ .
But, at the bottom of the monster-flanked steps
Jankan’s wrath was changed to jubilation. A
moon-faced yellow urchin, dodging through the
orange-trees of the court-yard, thrust upon him a
fomave d pih S p!l a f« v Vi an,shed -- p Jankan, reading,
forgave Pah-Pak, for here was Pah-Pak’s daughter
herself delivering over the white man to destruo-
tlon 9 #
A ~ . j . . ..
A belated sumpit-api, dropping from the
roof upon the slumbering Morgan’s face, promptly
expressed its feelings with its latter end. The
sailor’s sleep was a sailor’s, but since, if anything
could wake the dead, it would probably be the
fire-ant s ’ sting, Morgan was on his feet in an
instant—and not an instant too soon. Straight at
him, from the window, came a noiseless figure—a
shadow in the dim lamp-light—but a shadow with
a kris! A Chinese house is never short of
weapons-jars and vases without end—and from a
little bamboo table Morgan swung up a stately
piece of porcelain that knocked the stranger one
way and his kris another. There were smothered
voices outside; Morgan snatched up the kris and
rushed into the verandah. A glimmer of dawn
showed him a dozen men in the garden. “ Too
hot!” he said, and bounded across the flower-beds
towards the little door, standing open in the high,
mud wall. But a woman, close beside it, thrust
it sharply to, and was fumbling with the fasten-
ing when Morgan dragged her backwards by the
hair—Simi Lik’s hair !—flung her against the legs
of the pursuing Jankan, tore the door open, and
was off—running light in shirt and trousers.
After him, through the narrow mud-walled
streets, he could hear the shuffle of the naked
feet; no shouts—for the Malay can’t run with a
white man, and they wanted all their breath,
Instinctively, Morgan headed for the beach, till
unexpectedly he ran out of a winding lane
into sight of the long rollers gleaming to
the rapid equatorial sunrise. The hunted
man glanced at the crimson segment peering
over the sullen sea-line of the east—then at the
otnln^n P t“h7br k St i minS “* *
That Yankee s not more than half-a-mile out.”
He threw away the kris and dashed into the
surf, catching faintly the disappointed yell of the
human pack as he dived under the first advancing
wall of water.

ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Dear Bulletin,—“ Ung’a” maximum loads
for elephant, camel, and horse are for extended
marches. In the case I referred to, a nob very big
bull Baotrian was backed to get up with 1800
on him, travel 1800 yards, and lie down for un-
loading—all which he did without any apparent
distress. And the reason why-though a camel
can’t draw more than a powerful Clydesdale—he
can carry four times as much, is simply that his
spine, unlike the horse’s, curves slightly upwards
—making thus an enormous statical difference.
The Clydesdale’s legs would stand the camel’s
load—his back wouldn’t, because he “isn’t built
that way ! ”—Dromedary.

increased by the series of purchases made on behalf of English capitalists.
From the subjoined Reports by well-known mining authorities, there are two known reefs running
through the property, varying in size from Ito 2 feet, in a N.W. and S.E. course. There also appears
to be a third vertical reef, but upon which no work has been done up to the present.
The property also contains some very rich leaders, and doubtless some of these will be found to
junction in depth, giving place to another rich reef. It will be noticed that the original proprietor has
made the property pay from the start by merely dollying the stone in a primitive manner. The ad-
joining properties have also been paying in a similar way.
There is an ample supply of Mining and Milling timber in the immediate vicinity. As to water,
a most important factor in this district, there is a good and permanent supply obtainable from a lake
about three miles from the mine. Besides which, it is confidently expected that a sufficient supply will
be met with in sinking at a moderate depth.
It is estimated that the Capital to be placed to the credit of the Company, £5250, will be more
than ample to open up the mine, erect the necessary machinery, and place it in a dividend-paying
positi >n, when the smallness of the capital, £12,500, will tell most favourably in the results.
1 k The Company will be declared formed when 20,000 shares have been applied for.
After allotment of the shares, a meeting of allottees will be convened by the Provisional Directors
for the purpose of electing Directors and adopting Memorandum and Articles of Association.
The only Agreement for the sale of the property is one dated 12th May, 1894, between J. R.
M'Kenzie and others, which can be inspected at the Company’s Office, 131 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Application for Shares to be made on the accompanying form-to any of the above-named brokers.
